# Complete Guide to Excel Statistics with Copilot

> Data isn’t valuable until you put it to good use. Statistics transforms data into meaningful information, enabling organizations to make better decisions and predictions. That’s why statistics—collecting, analyzing, and presenting data—is a valuable skill for anyone in business or academia. In this course, statistics and business analysis instructor Eddie Davila takes you on a nuanced, step-by-ste

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/using-excel-for-statistics?u=76281980&t=0)** - Did your job just become all about data and [[Statistics]]? Do data-driven decisions need to be made and made fast? If you need to learn about stats and [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]], you've come to the right place. In this course, we'll learn how to understand and describe data sets. We'll explore [[Probability]] and then we'll dive into advanced topics like hypothesis tests and regression and to do all of this, we'll utilize the all-powerful statistical tool called Excel, plus we'll explore the possibilities with [[Microsoft Copilot|Copilot]]. Hi, I'm Eddie Davila and I love working with statistics and Excel. Now, if you're ready to get started, please join me here on [[LinkedIn]] Learning.

And while it'd be great for you to have even a tiny level of comfort with each, don't worry, you don't need to be especially good at either one. Having access to Excel and recognizing that stats exist everywhere around us, that'll be enough to get you started. Why? Because I'll be discussing some basic functions in Excel, teaching you how to navigate the cells and the menu. And also, I'll be explaining basic statistics concepts all the way through the course. Nonetheless, if you want a bit more comfort, consider watching parts of the Excel Essentials Training course and perhaps some of the Statistics Foundations 1 course. But if you just want to get started, let's just do it.

Whether you have a PC or a Mac, whether your version of Excel is a bit newer or older, most of what I'll do in Excel will be the same, or at least very similar to what you see on your version of Excel. But since most of the content in this course is foundational, I'm pretty sure you'll be able to follow along. One more really important thing to remember. With both Excel and [[Statistics]], there are usually multiple ways to arrive at the same solution. I'll usually show you a few ways to answer the question, but once your Excel skills begin to develop, you may find easier and more efficient ways to solve the problem.

#### [Excel basics](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/excel-basics?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/excel-basics?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Some of you may be [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] experts, others you can get by with your Excel skills, and some of you may be pretty new to Excel. So before we get started, let's go through some very basic Excel functions. Mostly these are things we're going to be using throughout the course. So this course I'll be recording on a Mac. Some of you may be on a PC. There are some slight differences from time to time. In some cases, when I say the [[Microsoft Word|word]] Command, for example, if I wanted to use the Command + D function on the Mac, that might be the Control + D function on the PC. But let's go ahead and move forward. In this case here, what I want to show you is the power of the equal sign. So the equal sign allows us to do a number of different things. For example, if I want to figure out what was in a different cell, so I can click equals, and then I can, let's say I wanted to copy what was in cell B3, so this right here, so I can type in B3. And there I go. And if this value were to change to 100, notice it changes what's in cell F3 as well. Let's change this back. So the other thing that we can do is we can add cells together. So we can create little formulas. So in this case here, I want to add what's in cell B3, and I want to add that to what's in cell B12. So that's the 10 in B12 and the 1 in B3. And that should give me 11, just like I was expecting.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/excel-basics?u=76281980&t=96)** Again, if this value here were to change to 25, that should change the value of what we have in that particular cell to 26. Let's change that back to 10. Now the other thing is, adding up all of those cells together, B3, B4, B5, all the way down to B12, that's going to take a while to type into Excel. So what I can do is see if there is a formula. And so there is a formula here, the Sum formula. So I'm going to type Sum, and notice it's already starts to populate down here. So I can click on Sum. And now it's asking me, well, what do you want to sum? And I can again click on these individually. But the other thing that I can do is, I can say, well, I want to add everything from B3, colon, down to B12. I close my parentheses, and there we go. It just added everything from B3 to B12. And once again, if this value were to change to 100, that should change the values both here and here. Now what we want to do here is we want to figure out what is the tax on each sale. So there's an 8% tax rate. And what I'm going to do here is I'm going to go ahead and going to say, well, the tax rate or the tax on this particular sale of $349 is going to be the $349 times the 8% tax rate.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/excel-basics?u=76281980&t=190)** So that gives me $27.92. Now the problem is that if I want to take that formula and I want to copy it down, I can go ahead and do that. But you'll notice this doesn't work out very well. So instead, what I'm going to do, and the reason this is happening is because the 8% is in this cell right here, B3, and Excel thought, well, since you want B3 here, you probably want B4 here and B5 here and B6. So that's what happened. Now we want to make sure that it locks in on B3, 'cause it's always going to be the tax rate of 8%. So I'm going to go ahead and do that. I'm going to make sure that it's locked in by going towards B3, and I always want it to B3. So I'm going to type in fn + f4. That's going to create an absolute cell, an absolute value. So it's not going to change from B3 ever again. And if I want to, I can do this manually, but I can also do fn + f4 and it's going to change it for me automatically. Now, it didn't change the value here, but watch what happens when we decide to copy down and notice what I can do here. I can click over this little handle, I'm going to click on that, drag it down, and notice in every case it's taking E5 plus the same tax rate, E6 plus this tax rate. And if the tax rate were to change to let's say 9%,
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/excel-basics?u=76281980&t=287)** all those values change. The other thing I want to show you here is that, while we don't want to do it necessarily is copy to the right, you can do that as well. And notice it's still locked in on B3. To find the actual total, the sale amount plus the tax, we would take this value here, add it to this value here, that gives me the total. And then once again, I can drag it down. But Excel is pretty smart. Notice what I just did there. Instead of dragging it down, which I could have done, I went to the handle and I double clicked and it knew that I wanted it to fill out the [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] of my table right there. In this case, what we want to do is we want to sum the pounds of potatoes, and then we want to move that over into a different table. So this is easy. We can type in Sum, and then I can take the sum of all of those values, and there we go. There's 70 pounds of potatoes. But if I copy this, and then paste it over here, you'll notice it's not 70 pounds anymore because it believes, and you'll look at the formula here, it's trying to say, well, if you wanted to add these five values, you must want to add these five values over here, which is nice in some cases, but in this particular case, I want to take the pounds of potatoes and put 'em there. So instead, what I can do, there's a couple options here.
>
> **[6:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/excel-basics?u=76281980&t=382)** One is I can copy and then I can do a Paste Special, and I can just put the value. This now changes this, and notice up here in the formula area, it changes it to a hardcore 70. You can't change it anymore. And the unfortunate thing about that is if we decide to change this to 20, there's actually 80 pounds of potatoes and notice this is locked in. So we don't want that. So I'm going to undo this. And instead what I can do is I can either say, well, the potatoes is going to be equal to whatever this cell is always. Another way is, remember we just looked at absolutes. So I can say I always want it to be B4 to B8. So if I click on this, again, fn + f4, then I go to B8, fn + f4,. That has locked in this formula. So it'll always be the sum of B4 to B8. I can now Copy, Paste, and if this number is to change, both this number and this number will change. The other thing you can do within Excel is you can turn a table into something a little prettier, right? An actual table. So by just being somewhere in the area of the data, I can hit, in the Mac, Command + T.
>
> **[7:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/excel-basics?u=76281980&t=477)** And now it asks me, is this the area you want to be your table? It is. And it also asks me whether or not the first row right here are my headers. Yeah, these are going to be my fruits and vegetables. This over here is the amount of pounds I have. So I click okay, and you'll notice it turned it into a nice little table and I can change the formatting up here as well. Lots of different options. Sometimes we're going to see that our data set is really big and moving around it could take a little bit of while. So scrolling down and scrolling up, that could take a while. So instead, what I can do is I can start using my Command and my Arrow. So notice I'm all the way on this far left side of my data set. What I can do is, well, how many rows are in this data set? So I can hit Command and the Down Arrow, it takes me all the way to the bottom. Oh, we're down to row 93. I can also use Command to the right. It takes me to the right far end of my dataset. Command + Arrow Up takes me all the way up to the header. And Command arrow to the left sort of takes me to just about where I started. Now by adding in just the Command + Shift, so Command + Shift and then the Right Arrow, it allows me to highlight the entire first row. Let's go back to the beginning here. I can do Command + Shift + Down. It now allows me to highlight the first column. And if I want to grab all of the data, I can hit Command + A and it allows me to grab everything.
>
> **[9:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/excel-basics?u=76281980&t=574)** Now, if I don't want that header, you might say, well, is there a way around that? So I can do Command + Shift, Right Arrow, then Down. And now it's gotten all of the numbers in the dataset. One last thing I want to show you is this cool trick that they call flash fill. Notice, in this case, I've gotten the first person on this list. I got their first name, I have their last name, and then it was put together in one particular cell. I want to do this with the rest of the names. And even though this is a short list, it could take me a while to type all of this and put it all together. So what I can do is I can see if Excel can figure this out for me. So I'm going to delete this here, and we're going to hit Control + E. If I hit Control + E, hey, Excel, do you know what's going on here? And look at that. It just figured out that I wanted the first name and last name put together with a space in between, in the full name column. There you go. All right, we're all set to use Excel.

Well, there are two functions often used to identify the middle of the dataset. The first is the mean, also known as the average. #### [Frequency table](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/frequency-table?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/frequency-table?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A frequency table is a very simple, but very helpful tool when you're trying to get to know your data set. It allows you to put your data points in the buckets. For example, with exam scores, it allows you to see how many students scored between 90 and 100, how many scored between 80 and 89, and so on and so forth. This table will then help us create a bar chart or histogram that represents the same distribution in graphical form. In this video, we'll focus on a few different ways you can create a frequency table in [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]]. In our next video, we'll use the same table to create the histogram. Okay, let's get started. So when we have a really small data set, as we see right here, five test scores, we don't really need a frequency table, do we? We can tell that there's one exam score in the 60s, one in the 70s, one in the 80s, and that there are two in the 90s. The issue is when we have 221 test scores and putting those into buckets of 90s, 80s, 70s, and so on. Now, what you can see that I've done right here is I've started to build at least a piece of our frequency table. And the first thing I did was I said, well, an A for this class is going to be a score between 90 and up to 100. For a B, it's going to be a score between 80 and up to 89. And all of my numbers in the exam score column,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/frequency-table?u=76281980&t=95)** they're all whole numbers. So we don't have any decimals here. And you'll notice that I've named the high end of each bucket or each grade, I've named that the bin. So we have a bin of 100, which means it's going to go from 100 down to the next number, which is 89. So that tells us it's from 90 to 100. Our bin of 89, that goes from 89 to 80 because our next bin begins at 79. So this is going to be important as we create our frequency table. For this, we're going to use the FREQUENCY function. So I can start typing in frequency, and there is our FREQUENCY function. The FREQUENCY function is asking us for two things, the data array, where are we going to get all of our test scores, and then our bin array, which you can see that we've highlighted already. So let's go ahead and choose our data array, and that's going to be all the numbers that we have right here. And then we're going to hit comma, and then we're going to utilize the bin array. But notice, the top bin goes from 90 to 100, and it's possible, because we stopped our highest bin at 100, it's possible that there could be an exam score above 100. If you want to double check this, here's another thing you can do. You can sum up the numbers in your frequency table. And what do we find? There are 221 test scores in our frequency table and 221 test scores in total that we've counted. Now, one thing I don't love about this particular way of developing a frequency table is the fact that we have this two set of cells, two columns here that are defining our bin
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/frequency-table?u=76281980&t=286)** from low of 90 and up to 100. How about if I want to do this in a way that maybe looks a little bit better visually? So for this frequency table, we're going to use a different function. We're going to use the COUNTIFS function, and it's very important that you notice that there are a couple of different COUNT functions, and we're not looking at COUNTIF, we're looking at COUNTIFS. And the reason why is because we want to check two things. Is it at the lowest 90 and at its highest 100? So here we go. We're going to first choose our criteria range with looking at our test scores. We use our quotation marks. We want this number to be greater than or equal to 90 on the low end. We close the quotation marks. Then I'm going to hit comma. And as we can see right up here, you'll notice I have typed this all in. I'm going to do this the easy way. I'm going to copy this and I'm going to paste. And so you can see it has copied it over again because I'm going to do the same thing, except I'm going to change what's in the parentheses where the first time I wanted to know is it greater than

> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/histogram?u=76281980&t=376)** And you can see my dotted lines are getting bigger here in the background. Can change the color. And you'll notice that some of them, they're green at the bottom and they kind of fade into gray at the top. Some people, once they have their histogram all put together, they don't like the histogram on the Excel background. So, the other thing that you can do is head up to the top where it says View. You can go to Gridlines, get rid of that, and now your histogram is highlighted nicely. All right, so hopefully that's helped you build your histogram. And as you can see, there's lots of different options, but these were a few options that most folks like to look at.

#### [Standard deviation](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/standard-deviation?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Lecturer] Means, medians and modes, max and min, they provide us some helpful information about our dataset. They help us identify the size, shape, and center of our dataset, but they definitely have their limitations, especially with large dataset. For example, as you can see, one massive rogue data point could create a huge range for a dataset that otherwise had limited variability. This is where standard deviation comes into play. Standard deviation is a measure of variation. It tells us how dispersed our data is from the mean. In other words, using a formula, it quantifies how far data point number one is from the mean. And then data point number two's distance from the mean and so on. A low standard deviation tells us the data points are mostly very close to the mean. A high standard deviation is trying to tell us the data points are all very spread out. So, you're probably wondering how do we calculate standard deviation? Well, to find the standard deviation, we must first find the variance. Here's the formula for the population variance, just in case we have all the data for a population. And you can also see here the sample variance in case you only have a sample from that population. First, it's obvious.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/standard-deviation?u=76281980&t=95)** Want you to notice how ugly these formulas are. Thankfully, we have [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] to do the heavy lifting. Also, I want you to notice both formulas result in the variance being a squared number. This is why we use standard deviation. The standard deviation is the square root of the variance, which means standard deviation is in the same units as our means and medians. And this will make it much easier to understand when we're trying to compare means and standard deviations. Let's head over to Excel to start calculating some standard deviations. #### [Normal curve and normal distribution](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/normal-curve-and-normal-distribution?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/normal-curve-and-normal-distribution?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The normal curve, you probably have an idea of what it looks like. Often it's referred to as the bell curve, and it's pretty easy to see why. But what exactly is the normal curve and what are some of its key characteristics? First, the curve represents all the possible outcomes in a distribution, a normal distribution. If we had the heights for all the people in a large city, we would expect the histogram to look similar to the normal curve. This is why we say the area under the curve is equal to one. It represents 100% of all the possible outcomes. Every person in the city is represented by that curve. And similar to our histograms, the higher points on the curve represent the most likely outcomes. Second, the true normal curve is symmetrical. The area on the right side of the mean is identical to the area on the left side of the mean. In other words, the two sides of the curve perfectly mirror each other around the mean, the center line. Third, since one half of the curve is the same as the other half, and since it's centered around the mean, the normal curve shows us that the mean and the median are the same. And since the tallest part of the curve is at the mean, this also tells us the mode is equal to the mean and the median.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/normal-curve-and-normal-distribution?u=76281980&t=93)** Fourth, in [[Statistics]], we say the perfect normal curve is centered at a mean of zero and has a standard deviation of one. You might be saying, well, the things we measure, for example, people's heights, test scores, and the time it takes people to run marathons, these don't have a mean of zero. This is why we often standardize our data. In other words, we convert each height, test score, and run time into a value that fits on the normal curve. If I just confused you, don't worry, we'll be covering that with z-scores in later videos. Finally, you might be thinking: What in the real world could actually fit the normal curve? I mean, the requirements for having a normal distribution seem pretty strict. Well, believe it or not, in nature, many large data sets are said to be normally distributed. So whether it's the height of oak trees, the weight of apples, the heights or weights of people, we would expect these populations to have normally distributed data. As we move forward, you'll begin to see that if we took a sample of 100 test scores, arrange them from smallest to largest, put them on a histogram, we would expect the histogram to sort of look like a normal curve. And the more test scores we have, in other words, the larger our sample size, the more and more we would expect our histogram to look like the normal curve.

> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Statistics]] (1)
> **Definitions:** in other words (3)
> **Analogies:** similar to (1), for example (1)

#### [Skewness and kurtosis](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/skewness-and-kurtosis?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/skewness-and-kurtosis?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Mean, median, mode, even standard deviation. In most cases, these words sound pretty familiar, even for those of us that are new to [[Statistics]]. But how about skewness and kurtosis? Those might be new concepts for most of you. So, let's see how these two statistical concepts can be useful in trying to understand our dataset. As we've already discussed, in nature, data often takes the shape of the normal curve, a curve that is symmetrical on each side of the mean, a curve where the mean, the median, and the mode are identical. And the normal curve is often presented as being centered at a mean of zero and having a standard deviation of one. Also, as has been discussed earlier in this course, in nature, datasets for a population, they very often reflect the normal distribution. Many population datasets look very much like the normal curve. All this being said, our sample datasets and sometimes even a population dataset, they may not perfectly fit the normal distribution, especially when the sample size is small. But often, even when we have a larger sample size, some distributions are more flat, others taller, some drift to the left, others drift to the right, and this, this is where skewness and kurtosis can be useful
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/skewness-and-kurtosis?u=76281980&t=95)** in helping us understand our dataset. How? Well, let's begin with skewness. Remember, a normal distribution has a curve that is symmetrical around the mean. Well, skewness measures how symmetrical our data is. In other words, are there more data points on the right side of the mean or the left side of the mean? A normal curve has a skewness rating of zero. If the skewness measures above zero, if it's positive, we say the data is positively skewed. There's a big number in the long tail on the right, the positive direction. If the skewness measures below zero, if skewness is negative, we say the data is negatively skewed. There's a really small number on the left side of the curve, the negative direction. Remember, a skewness of zero means our data is symmetrical on either side of the mean. Now, if skewness is between -0.5 and up to +0.5,
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/skewness-and-kurtosis?u=76281980&t=164)** this is generally considered a fairly symmetrical dataset. But suppose it's larger in magnitude, if it's between 0.5 and 1.0, or if it's between -0.5 and -1.0, this is often referred to as moderately skewed. And skewness above +1.0 or below -1.0, our dataset is considered extremely skewed. Now, let's look at kurtosis. Our normal curve with its bell shape is pretty distinctive, tall, but not too tall, wide, but not too wide. It has a kurtosis of zero. In relation to the normal curve, kurtosis is trying to see if we're getting more data points near our mean. This would give us a skinnier, taller curve. This would mean we have a positive kurtosis, a kurtosis above zero. On the other hand, if some of our data points that we would expect to be in the fat part of the curve near the mean, if they start showing up in the long tails instead, this results in a flatter curve, a shorter curve. This would mean we have a negative kurtosis, a kurtosis below zero. By the way, you may be wondering, "How are skewness and kurtosis calculated?" As you can see, neither is very simple, which is why [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] is so useful.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/skewness-and-kurtosis?u=76281980&t=259)** Not only does it calculate both of these statistics for us, it allows us to see how changing some data points in a dataset can change the skewness and kurtosis of a dataset. Let's examine the skewness and kurtosis of some datasets using Excel. So, here we are back with some of our smaller datasets, and we want to find the skewness and kurtosis. So, we start typing in skew, and there you go. We're looking for the skew of a dataset, not for a population. So, we'll type in skew. And we're going to take the skewness of these five data points. We'll do the same here with kurtosis. And there we go. Our kurtosis for these five data points. And look at what we have here. First of all, our skewness is zero, which is very much like the normal curve. And that kind of makes sense because if we look at our data points, we have 80, 70, 60, 40, very similar, at least in shape, to our normal curve. So, that's looking pretty good. Our kurtosis, though, is -1.2. So, this is telling us that if we were to put this into graphical form, we would expect our normal curve, actually, it's not a normal curve, we would expect our curve to be a little bit flatter than the normal curve. So, let's look at our second dataset. Again, our skewness is zero.
>
> **[5:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/skewness-and-kurtosis?u=76281980&t=353)** The numbers look like they are nicely distributed from left to right, but our kurtosis here is two. And so what this is telling us is that we have a much taller center than we would normally expect in a normal curve. And that makes sense, doesn't it? When we look at our mode, when we look at our median, it's pretty tall. There's three data points at 60, and then there's two around it, one at 55 and one at 65. So, again, s skewness and kurtosis doing their work.
>
> **[6:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/skewness-and-kurtosis?u=76281980&t=390)** What are we seeing here? Skewness, again, zero, very nicely centered around our median and mean, but we can see that we have a number of data points that are much higher and much lower than what we would normally expect. It's really stretching out that curve, flattening it, and that's why our kurtosis is -3, very flat. Notice what we have here, our skewness and our kurtosis doesn't give us really much of anything. And when we look at the data, we can see why. All of our data points are at the mean and median, and we don't have anything outside of that. So, it really doesn't know what to tell us. It's just telling us, look, this doesn't look anything like a normal curve. And when we look at the other data, the mean, the median, the mode, our variance and standard deviation, it's sort of easy to tell why. Let's go down here. Okay, so now we have a skewness of -.223. Does that make sense? Well, it does, because it's telling us that we have a number very far to the left that's throwing our symmetry off, if that makes sense. Now the other thing I want you to notice here is when our skewness gets to be pretty big, when our skewness is below -1 or above +1, well, our curve is so skewed that kurtosis isn't really helpful. And we already know this doesn't look very much
>
> **[8:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/skewness-and-kurtosis?u=76281980&t=483)** like a normal curve at all because it is so skewed. Our skew becomes very positive. Let's move over though. What does skewness look like here and our kurtosis?
>
> **[8:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/skewness-and-kurtosis?u=76281980&t=531)** So, as we get more data points, we start to see that our skewness and kurtosis start to vary a little bit more. #### [Empirical rule](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/empirical-rule?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/empirical-rule?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] As was discussed earlier in this course, the normal distribution, it pops up in all sorts of ways in nature. Heights, weights, test scores, blood pressure. In those types of circumstances, when we have those types of datasets, we often assume that the population is normally distributed, in which case, we would expect our data to follow the empirical rule. The empirical rule states that we should expect 68% of our data to be within one standard deviation of the mean, 95% of our data to be within two standard deviations of the mean, and 99.7% of our data to be within three standard deviations of the mean. So only 0.3% of data points are likely to be farther than three standard deviations from the mean. Again, I must remind you, this works when we have the well-centered, symmetrical, bell-shaped curve. Unfortunately, the empirical rule begins to lose value the farther our dataset strays from the classic normal distribution. But don't be too concerned. You'll be surprised just how well the empirical rule holds up. So the next question is, how can we tell the distance from any one single data point to the mean? For that, we calculate something called a z-score. The formula for our z-score is seen here: the value of our data point, x,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/empirical-rule?u=76281980&t=95)** minus our average for the sample, mu, divided by the standard deviation of our sample, sigma. And as I said, I would expect 68% of my data points to have z-scores between negative one and positive one. 95% are expected to have z-scores between negative two and positive two. And 99.7% are expected to have z-scores between negative three and positive three. Only 0.3% of our data points are expected to be outside of that range. Those data points are sometimes referred to as outliers. So let's head over to [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] and see the empirical rule in action. So here we're going to be looking at datasets that come from the real world, whether it be rainfall, the height of NBA players, or exam test scores. Let's begin with rainfall. First thing that we want to do here is we want to start to understand what we're looking for. We can see that we have rainfall data for 17 years. And we're expecting 68% of our 17 years to fall within one standard deviation of our mean. So let's go ahead and put that expectation right here. So we're expecting about 11.56, about 11 or 12, to fall within one standard deviation of our mean. And we can do a ctrl + e here,
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/empirical-rule?u=76281980&t=190)** and that's going to pick up the 95 and 99.7%. Let's go ahead and increase our decimal. There we go. And then, this, we can just copy this down. And so we can copy this as well. So our expectation is that 11.5 of the 17 years of data are expected to fall within one standard deviation of the mean. 16 of our data points should fall within two standard deviations of the mean. And pretty much every data point should fall within three standard deviations of the mean. So for this, we now need to find our z-score. So our z-score is our, and we'll open our parentheses here, is our data point, in this case, 42.88, minus our mean. But we're going to be using this formula over and over again, so we'll want to turn that into an absolute value. So we'll use fn + f4 on our Mac, close parentheses. And then we're going to divide that by our standard deviation. And again, we're going to be using that same standard deviation over and over again, so we'll turn that also into an absolute value. We can now see our z-score right here. And we can either copy down this way or any way you'd like. And you'll notice, we have z-scores for all 17 of our years. Now, the thing I like to do to make this easier on myself to count up how many fall within one, two, or three standard deviations of the mean
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/empirical-rule?u=76281980&t=284)** is to turn these z-scores into absolute z-scores. And by that I mean turning all of our z-scores into positive numbers. And there's a formula for this as well. Absolute, ABS, and this number. And if it's already a positive number, it will stay a positive number. And if it's a negative number, like we see right here, it turns it into a positive number. I now copy this down. And I can arrange these from smallest to largest. So let's take a look. We're expecting about 11 to fall within one standard deviation. What do we have here? 10 of these data points have fallen within one standard deviation of the mean. And if you are wondering how I counted those up, notice this little box right here. When I highlight things, it tells me that I've captured 10 rows. Now we're expecting 16 to fall within two standard deviations. So we keep scrolling down and there it is, 16 data points have fallen within two standard deviations of the mean. And all 17 have fallen within three standard deviations of the mean. So again, nature likes to normal distribution. Let's take a look at the same thing with NBA heights. So once again, I've done a lot of the math here for you. 68%, we have 100 NBA players and their heights here in centimeters. We have the mean. We have our standard deviation. We're expecting 68 of our 100 players
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/empirical-rule?u=76281980&t=378)** to fall within one standard deviation, 95 to fall within two standard deviations, and pretty much everybody to fall within three standard deviations. So the first thing I do is I go ahead and I find my z-score. So again, my z-score is the B2 minus my average. And I want to turn that into an absolute value. And we're going to divide that by our standard deviation. Also, turn that into absolute value. And I'm going to copy this down by double-clicking here. And then, my absolute z-score, and you can notice these are all arranged by smallest to largest. We want to do it by player ID, but we can do it this way as well. We were expecting about 781. And we did have one person that was an outlier. In this case, this student had a very low score. They were 3.49 standard deviations away from the mean. So they would be considered an outlier as well. All right, so hopefully this helps you see a couple different things. First of all, the power of the empirical rule. Second, the fact that so many things in nature really gravitate towards the normal distribution and can be very helpful in helping us identify some outliers.

#### [Probability explained](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/probability-explained?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/probability-explained?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Nobody has all the answers, but it seems everyone can tell you the [[Probability]]. There's a 50% chance that in a single roll, you will roll either a four, five, or six with the die. There's a 73% chance LeBron James will make this next free throw. There's a 99% chance my boss will be drinking coffee at this meeting. Three probabilities each is a little different. There's a 50% chance that in a single roll I will roll either a four, five, or six with the die. This is what we call classical probability. We know the die has six sides. We know that if we roll the die each outcome, one through six is equally likely. Four, five and six are half of the possible outcome. So we are confident that the 50% probability is accurate. There is a 73% chance LeBron James will make this free throw. This is what we call empirical probability. Based on LeBron's lifetime NBA [[Statistics]], we believe there is a 73% chance he will make this next free throw. Look, there's plenty of past data to look at, but perhaps tonight he's tired. Perhaps he's feeling great and more focused than ever, or maybe he's a little bit injured. Nonetheless, the data we have provides a credible probability. There is a 99% chance my boss will be drinking
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/probability-explained?u=76281980&t=96)** coffee at this meeting. This probability is not based on statistics, but rather on personal belief. This person seems to be very confident their boss will have a coffee at the meeting, but they're still saying there's a tiny chance they will not have a coffee. It's not exactly statistically sound, but it does demonstrate their confidence in this probability. Also, consider that some events have even odds, which means that every outcome is equally likely rolling a one with a die or rolling a two with a die, both equally likely. Picking a queen out of a 52 car deck or picking a jack out of a 52 car deck, as long as the deck is complete, the odds are the same. Flipping a coin heads or tails, either outcome is equally likely. On the other hand, in the desert of Phoenix, Arizona, the chance it will rain on any given day of the year is about 10%. It only rains about 36 days per year, which means the odds of no rain are about 90%. Those are definitely not even odds. Let's begin to play around with probabilities in [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]]. #### [Permutations](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/permutations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/permutations?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] What are the chances of rolling a six-sided die and getting a four? There's only one single four on the die, and there are six sides, so you have a one in six chance. But what's the [[Probability]] of choosing a queen from a standard deck of 52 cards? In this case, there's four queens in a deck, and a deck has 52 cards. So, if you pick only one card at random from the full deck, your chances are four divided by 52, 7.7%. This was easy. We knew there were four winning cards, four queens, and we knew that there were 52 cards in the deck. But sometimes when you're trying to calculate probability in the real world, things are a little bit more complex. For example, suppose we have a group of 10 people in a race, the three fastest runners, first, second, and third place, they'll each get a prize. How many different possible first, second, and third place combinations are there if we have 10 runners? Actually the correct [[Microsoft Word|word]] we're looking for here is permutations. Permutations are the number of ways in which objects can be arranged. Let's begin easy. Suppose we only had three runners: Maya, Ari, Elaine. How many first, second, and third place permutations would we have?
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/permutations?u=76281980&t=94)** Here they are, six. Sure, it might have taken you a bit of time to figure out all six permutations, but eventually, you can figure them out. But what if I didn't want to sit down and struggle to make this list? And what if it wasn't just three runners? Well, we have a formula n, exclamation point. Actually, we call this n factorial. What does it mean? Well, let's say we wanted to find the number of different permutations for five runners, from first place to fifth place? We use five factorial, which means we multiply all the integers from five down to one. So five factorials equal to five times, four times, three times, two times one. Yep. Believe it or not, there are 120 different ways in which these five contestants can finish this contest. But let's go back to our original question. Suppose we have a group of 10 people in a race. The three fastest runners, first, second, and third place, will get a prize. How many different possible first, second, and third place permutations are there if we have 10 runners? Here, we use a different formula: N factorial divided by the quantity, N minus X factorial. Where N is the total number of objects. In this case, that would be our 10 runners, and X is the number of objects to be selected.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/permutations?u=76281980&t=188)** In this case, we're interested in the top three finishers. So X is equal to three. So we end up with 10 factorial divided by seven factorial. Notice, we have three, two, and one, both at the top and the bottom, so we can cancel those out. So we end up with 10 times, nine times, eight times, seven times, six times five, and times four. 604,800 different permutations for those top three spots when we have 10 runners. So let's move to [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] and look at some permutations. As you might expect, Excel has a permutations function. Let's do it with something really easy first. Here we have three objects, A, B, and C, and we want to know the number of permutations for those three objects, and we know that it's already six, but let's go over here and we'll type in PERM. And there we see the permute, permutations function. We click on this and we want three. So there are three items, and we're going to choose all three of them. And so we type this in and it's going to do the calculation for us, six, just as we expected. So let's do a relatively simple problem. So let's say that there is a contest, a raffle, and there are eight people that can win,
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/permutations?u=76281980&t=284)** and first, second, and third place will each get a prize, but they're going to get different prizes. So the order in which these folks win prizes is going to be very important in terms of what they end up receiving. And the first thing that we want to do is figure out, well, how many different permutations are there for first, second, and third? Because ultimately we want to find out what's the probability that Kiki will win first place? So let's go ahead and first figure out total number of permutations. So we are going to type in our permutation. There's our permutation function. In this case, we have eight contestants, and we're going to choose three that get the prize. There are 336 permutations for those three prizes among these eight people. Now, if Kiki was to win first place, as we can see right here, and let's go ahead and put her name here as well. So Kiki wins first place. How many total permutations are there of Kiki winning first place? Well, in this case, we now have seven people left trying to win those last two prizes. So again, we can do permutation, but how many permutations are there if Kiki has already been awarded first place? Well, there's now seven contestants left that can win those two prizes. So there are 42 permutations in which Kiki
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/permutations?u=76281980&t=380)** wins first place. What are the odds? What's the probability that Kiki wins first place? Well, there are 42 ways in which Kiki can win, and we're going to divide that by the total number of permutations, and we can see there's a 12.5% that Kiki wins first place. Now we overcomplicated this problem, but we did get to see along the way, that there are very good uses for permutations, especially when we start seeing some more complex questions being asked.

#### [Addition rule of probability](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/addition-rule-of-probability?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/addition-rule-of-probability?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Speaker] Let's say you have two coins, we flip both coins. What are the odds that the coin flips will result in at least one coin resulting in heads? To solve this [[Probability]] problem, we're going to use something called the addition rule of probability. The addition rule of probability is often presented like this. The probability of A or B is equal to the probability of A plus the probability of B minus the probability of both A and B. Confused? Let's go back to our two coins. What do we want? We want at least one of the two coins to result in heads. Let's say probability A is the probability that coin number one results in heads. That's 50%. Let's say that probability B is the probability that coin number two results in heads. That also is 50%, but what is the probability that both coin A and coin B will result in heads? This is what we call the probability of both A and B. Well, here are all the possible outcomes of the two coin flips. Heads on flip number one and tails on flip number two. Tails on flip one and heads on flip two. Tails on both flips. Heads on both flips.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/addition-rule-of-probability?u=76281980&t=93)** As you can see, one of four outcomes results in both coins as heads. One of four, the probability of both A and B is 25%. So the probability of either coin resulting in heads is the probability of A, 50% plus the probability of B, 50%
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/addition-rule-of-probability?u=76281980&t=119)** minus the probability of both A and B, 25%. There is a 75% chance that at least one coin will result in heads, which is exactly what we see here. Three of the four possible outcomes, 75% of our outcomes have at least one heads. Let's head over to [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] and put the addition rule to work. So here, we can see exam score data for a number of students. And as we can see, they have test scores for both exam number one and exam number two. Now, we want to find out is what is the probability that any one student got at least 90 points or higher on one of the exams, so you don't have to get 90 or higher on both exams, just on one of the exams. So the first thing we want to do is we want to figure out, well, how many students are there? How many people could actually get 90 plus points on both exams? So for this, we're going to count the students and what we can do here is use a count A 'cause we want to see how many different students we have. Now we do have a heading here, so we'll want to subtract the heading, but you can see we have 786 students. And if we scroll down and we want to make sure, should take us down to 787, which is what we would expect when our top cell is the label.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/addition-rule-of-probability?u=76281980&t=219)** So we have 786 students. So we're going to take a look at all of these numbers here, and our criteria is we put this in quotes that the score B greater than or equal to 90. We close our quotes, we close our parentheses, and we find that 112 students got a score of 90 or higher on exam number one. Let's do the same thing for exam number two, COUNTIF,
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/addition-rule-of-probability?u=76281980&t=275)** and we're going to take exam score two, all the scores here, we want to make sure that each score is greater than or equal to 90. We close our quotes, we close our parentheses. We had 110 students that got a 90 points or higher on exam two. And at this point, we can start figuring out these probabilities. So we had 112 students, 112 occurrences out of 786 students. So our probability here is about 14%. For exam two, very similar number. So we're expecting something similar here. 110 occurrences, 786 total students. And once again, we can see that we are very close to about 14%. Let's now find the total number of students that have both a score of 90 plus on exam one and also on exam two. For this, we're going to use a different formula, instead of COUNTIF, we're going to use COUNTIFS, and we're going to be checking two criteria. First of all, did this student get a score of 90 or higher on the first exam? So we're going to check this by doing greater than or equal to 90, and I close my parentheses.
>
> **[6:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/addition-rule-of-probability?u=76281980&t=370)** Then I'm going to do something very similar except instead of doing it for exam one, I want to do this for exam two. Once again, I'm going to put in my test here of greater than or equal to a score of 90. I closed my parentheses and we had 20 students, 20 total students that got a score of 90 on both exams. So if we had 20 students that got a 90 or higher on both exams out of 786, that is an overlap of about 2%. We can change all of these to percentages if we'd like.
>
> **[7:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/addition-rule-of-probability?u=76281980&t=427)** So now, we put the addition rule into action. We now have the probability of A, a student got a 90% or higher on the first exam. To that, we add the probability of B. A student got 90 or higher on the second exam and we subtract the overlap, the students that got 90 or higher on both exam one and exam two. And what do we find? The probability that a student got a 90 on at least one exam out of these two is 25.7%. So we were able to see the addition rule in action, the probability of A plus the probability of B minus the probability of both A and B.

#### [Multiplication rule of probability](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/multiplication-rule-of-probability?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/multiplication-rule-of-probability?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We have two dice, one red, one white. What are the odds we roll a six with both dice? Well, the odds of rolling a six on the red die is one in six, 16.7%. The odds of rolling a six on the white die is also one in six, 16.7%. To find the odds of rolling a six with both dice, we use the multiplication rule. We multiply the [[Probability]] for each independent event, 16.7% times 16.7%. This tells us there is a 2.79% chance both dice will result in six. By the way, when we see a grid of all the possible outcomes, we see that there are 36 possible outcomes. Only one of those is double six. One divided by 36 is 2.79%. But let's consider a different scenario. Suppose I have a standard deck of 52 cards. I draw three cards. I will choose one card at a time. What's the probability all three cards will either be a 10, jack, queen, king, or ace? Let's use [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] and the multiplication rule to help us solve this problem. So in our file right here, we can see that I've already pre-populated 10 Jack, queen, king, and ace.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/multiplication-rule-of-probability?u=76281980&t=93)** And we know that there are four of each of these in the deck, diamond, spades, clubs, and hearts. So there are four 10s, four jacks, queens, kings, and aces, and I'm just going to copy across, copy to the right. And so that means, and we know that there are 52 cards in a full deck of cards. So when we're looking to do probabilities, we're looking at the favorable outcomes divided by the total possible outcomes. So in this case right here, the favorable outcomes, well, we're looking for 10, Jack, queen, king, or ace. And we're going to sum these up. So there are 20 favorable outcomes in the deck and a total of 52 cards in the deck, so the probability of drawing a 10 through ace on the first card is simply just the outcomes divided by the number of cards in the deck. So it's about 38%, 38.46%. We're going to do the same thing here for card number two, but we have to realize that one card has already been taken out of the deck, and we're going to assume that that card was a 10 through ace. So we're going to take this number right here, the 20, and we're going to subtract one from that, and we're going to do the same thing here because there are no longer 52 cards in the deck. There are now 51, so I'm going to just copy this down because that's going to be the B9 minus one of the cards.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/multiplication-rule-of-probability?u=76281980&t=187)** And we can do the same thing here. We can copy this formula because we're going to take the 19 divided by 51, and the odds that we will pick a 10 through ace on that second card have dropped to 37.25%. Again, we can do the same thing for the third card, same formula. We're going to take this number and reduce it by one,
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/multiplication-rule-of-probability?u=76281980&t=212)** and we'll do the same here. We no longer have 51 cards in the deck. We now have 50 cards in the deck. Once again, we want to take the probability, there are 18 10 through aces left in the deck, only 50 cards left in the deck, and so there's a 36% chance that we will draw a 10 through ace on that third card, assuming that we've done that for card number one and card number two. So we can now use the multiplication rule. The probability that the first three cards we pull from the deck are either a 10 jack, queen, king, or ace is going to be 38.46 times 37.25 times 36%, and what we can do is we can do the product function. So you can see right here, and we can multiply all of these together. And what we find is that there is a 5.16% chance that the first three cards we will choose from the top of the deck are going to be either a 10, jack, queen, king, or ace. All right, that's the multiplication rule. Pretty simple, but very helpful.

#### [Conditional probability and probability trees](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/conditional-probability-and-probability-trees?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/conditional-probability-and-probability-trees?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] [[Probability]] trees, a really useful tool when you have a series of probability events. For example, let's say we are flipping a coin, our two possible outcomes, heads and tails, each represents a different branch on our probability tree. Each branch is labeled with both the outcome and also the probability. But that was just coin flip number one, whether we got heads or tails on coin flip number one, we flip coin number two. So from each of our coin number one branches, we create a new set of branches to represent flip number two. Each new set presents us the probabilities of coin Flip number two, heads and tails, in both cases, with a 50% probability. Follow the branches from left to right. At the end of each branch, you'll find the end result of the two flips. The top set of branches leads us to HH, heads, heads. If we multiply all the probabilities along those branches, 50% and 50%, it gives us the probability of heads, heads, 25%. You can do the same for each of the other possibilities. The other great thing about probability trees is that we can measure how probability changes after each outcome. In other words, if the result of flip number one is heads, we now know the probability of flipping heads twice in a row just went from 25% before flip number one to 50%

### 7. Discrete Probabilities

#### [Discrete probability explained](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/discrete-probability-explained?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/discrete-probability-explained?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] For these next few sections, there are three new terms we need to understand. Random variable, discrete, and continuous. Let's begin with random variable. Why random? Why do we use that [[Microsoft Word|word]]? Well, as we perform experiments, we need to understand that the value of the eventual outcome of the experiment is unknown or random. This is why we call the result of an experiment a random variable. So the sum of dots on a single roll of two dice is a random variable. The amount of rain that will fall in New York this month is a random variable. The length of time you will wait in line at Starbucks tomorrow, that too, is a random variable. As is the total number of drinks the 10th customer of the day orders. These random variables can either be discrete random variables or continuous random variables. Let's begin with discrete random variables. The number of drinks the next Starbucks customer will order is very likely as low as zero. Perhaps they just want to order a food item, but probably no larger than 10. And since they can't order half drinks, the outcome has to be a whole number. This is an example of a discrete random variable. The same thing goes for the sum of dots on a single roll of two dice. The only possibilities are two through 12. Again, we do not have any decimals.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/discrete-probability-explained?u=76281980&t=95)** This too would be a discrete [[Probability]]. So what are continuous random variables? Consider the amount of rainfall in New York for the month of October. It might end up being 0.58 inches, 2.35 inches. It might even be 4.77 inches. There really is no end to the possible rain outcomes for October. The same goes for your wait at Starbucks tomorrow. You might wait 36 seconds, four minutes and 17 seconds, or perhaps they are very busy, and you end up waiting 10 minutes and 33 seconds. Again, the possibilities are endless. Continuous variables don't have to be whole numbers. They can be any value. And this distinction between discrete and continuous is important in determining probabilities. Let's start with our dice. A nice and predictable experiment. Probability tells us the distribution of possible outcomes would look like this. And here in this frequency table is the distribution of drinks ordered by each customer at one store during a two hour period. There are limited number of possible outcomes, and because of this, the frequency table is very helpful. On the other hand, here is a table of rainfall in New York for the last 10 Octobers. The same number doesn't even come up once.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/discrete-probability-explained?u=76281980&t=189)** Discrete random variables have fairly limited outcomes. Random variables have limitless possible outcomes. Thus each will require different probability techniques. But before we delve into discrete and continuous random variable probabilities, look around you. Consider your place of work. Think of your favorite sports. Think about environmental data. Can you identify discrete random variables and continuous random variables in your life and in the world around you? And as you start to think about that, start to think about how probabilities are calculated and discussed differently in each scenario.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Probability]] (3), [[Microsoft Word|Word]] (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (4), is an  (1)
> **Versions:** 0.58 (1), 2.35 (1), 4.77 (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Discrete: Mean](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/discrete-mean?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/discrete-mean?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Imagine a study that is tracking the number of drinks each individual customer orders at a popular local cafe between 10:00 a.m and 12:00 p.m. This study is tracking discrete random variables. Each customer's order is presently unknown. Thus, each order will give us a random variable. And each customer will order a discrete number of drinks, one, two, three, six, perhaps even zero if they are just ordering food. The important thing to realize is that all orders will result in a discrete number of drinks, whole numbers, integers, no fractions or decimals. You can't order 0.35 drinks. Let's head over to [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] and look at the data collected in this study. As you can see, we've been given a frequency table. The frequency table has told us here that three customers in this two-hour period ordered zero drinks, 24 customers ordered one drink, and so on. And so what we want to find out is what's the average number of drinks each customer ordered? So the first thing we want to do is we want to add up the total number of customers that we have. So we'll sum all of our customers here, and it looks like we had 56 customers in this two-hour period at this local cafe. The next thing we want to do
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/discrete-mean?u=76281980&t=92)** is we want to figure out the relative frequency. So how many customers ordered zero drinks? And so what we're going to do for this is we're going to say, well, three customers ordered zero drinks out of 56. So we're going to divide the number of customers that ordered zero drinks, which is three customers, by our total number of customers. Now, we're going to want to copy this formula down, so we're going to turn that into the absolute value. We're going to put in our dollar signs here. And we find that 5.4% of customers ordered zero drinks. We can then copy down. 43% of customers roughly ordered one drink. 32% of customers ordered two drinks. And again, we'll just copy it all the way down. And if we want to make sure that everything is okay, we can sum these and it's 100%. So that's just telling us that everyone's been accounted for because 5.4% plus 43% all the way down to 1.8% is going to add up to 100%. The next thing we want to calculate is the weight. So at this point, what we're going to do is we're going to take our relative frequency. 5.4% of customers ordered zero drinks,
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/discrete-mean?u=76281980&t=187)** so that will be our weight. The number of drinks ordered times are relative frequency, and we'll do the same thing here. The number of drinks ordered, which is now one, times a relative frequency of 43%. And as we copy this down, it now shows us that two drinks at 32% gives us a weight of 0.64. Three six drinks at a rate of 1.8% gives us a weight of 0.107. So what we do with these weights is we add them together. When we add these together, we get an answer of 1.77. This is our discrete mean. Between the hours of 10:00 a.m. and 12:00 p.m., the average customer ordered 1.77 drinks in their order.

#### [Discrete: Binomial distribution](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/discrete-binomial-distribution?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/discrete-binomial-distribution?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When an experiment has only two possible outcomes, the result is what we call a binomial random variable. A coin flip can only result in heads and tails. A patient can either test positive or negative for a disease. These are possible binomial random variables, provided we have n trials with the [[Probability]] of success we call P. In other words, if we take our coin and flip it four times, n is equal to 4, the number of flips. P is 0.50, the chance of success, which can either be heads or tails in this case. Consider these two problems. First, a town is calling community members for jury duty. These are the folks that might eventually be chosen to serve as members of a jury. Let's say 20 people from the community will be chosen for a jury pool at random. The jury pool is selected from a community that is 55% female. In this situation, n is equal to 20, and P is equal to 0.55 or 55%. What's the probability only five members of the 20-person jury pool will be female? Second question, a certain club invites people to attend their meetings in the hopes that people will join the organization. New people attend the meeting each month, but only 20% of the new people
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/discrete-binomial-distribution?u=76281980&t=94)** end up joining the organization. Suppose three people attend this month's meeting, Harry, Imani, and Luna. What are the chances one of these three people will join the organization? It's important to clarify our question. What are the chances that one, not more than one, not less than one, will join the organization. While we could try and solve both problems through some pretty ugly calculations, another way to solve these types of problems is with the help of a binomial probability table. Here's a small segment of one of those charts. We can solve the club membership problem using this table. Along the left side, you'll see N, the number of trials. We have three people, so N is equal to three. K is the number of successful trials. Remember, we want exactly one of the three new people to join. K is equal to 1. Next in the row with K = 1, we move to the right until we get to 0.20. That was the percentage of new people that joined the club. We find that there is a 38.4% chance exactly one person will join the club. In case you're wondering how that number is generated, remember we have three people. Person number one will join. This has a probability of 20%. Person number two will not join. This has a probability of 80%. Person number three will also not join.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/discrete-binomial-distribution?u=76281980&t=188)** This has a probability of 80%. We multiply these three probabilities, and we get 0.128, 12.8%. But remember, any one person can join. So each person has a 12.8% chance of joining. Three times 12.8% is 38.4%, which is the value on our table. But this table is already a bit ugly and it only goes up to N = 4. Luckily, we have [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]], and Excel can be very useful in working with binomial distributions. So in the file you've been provided, I've already pre-populated some things in here for you. For our example, and it's not one of the questions that we are going to answer, but for building the formulas, we're going to say that p is equal to 0.3, 30% chance of success, and we're going to have 14 trials. And what we want to do is we want to avoid those charts that are limiting us because they're so long and we need so many of them and build our own charts in here using a formula. And if we start typing binomial, you'll notice that we have a B-I-N-O-M.D-I-S-T, BINOM.DIST. This is the formula that we're going to want to use, and it's asking us to fill in four different things, and you can sort of see them along the right side there. First, it's the number of successful trials.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/discrete-binomial-distribution?u=76281980&t=284)** Well, we have this in our first column here, so we're going to just click on cell C6. The number of trials, we have that up here. That's n. We're going to start out with 14, but remember, we're going to be using this over and over again, so I want to add my dollar signs to that. The next thing is what is the probability of success? And we know that also. That is p is equal to 0.3. Click up here. We put our dollar signs on that because we'll be using that over and over again. And the next thing is it's asking us, "Do we want to add all these up cumulatively, or do we just want this one individual?" And we're going to click FALSE here 'cause we just want individual probability for now. And I close this up, and here we go. And then I'm going to copy this down. The probability that we will get exactly three successful trials is 19.43%. Now we can also create a cumulative probability. #### [Continuous: Z-score tables](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/continuous-z-score-tables?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/continuous-z-score-tables?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] What do we know so far? We know normal distribution is a continuous distribution. Typically it's illustrated via the normal curve. We also know that Z-scores provide a relative distance from the mean. A data point with a Z-score of 2.0 is two standard deviations above the mean. We've also learned about the power of the empirical rule and we learned how to calculate Z-scores for individual data points in our data sets. Those Z-scores can tell us how far that data point is from the mean, but Z-scores also allow us to calculate probabilities in a continuous distribution. For example, we know 68% of data points are within one standard deviation of the mean, which tells us that 32% of data points are outside of that area. Because the normal curve is symmetrical, we know 16% of those data points are more than one standard deviation from the mean in the positive direction, and 16% are more than one standard deviation from the mean in the negative direction. In order to find these probabilities for other Z-scores though, we usually need the help of something we call a Z-score table. Here you can see a portion of a Z-score table. How do you read this chart? Suppose we have a Z-score of 0.99. We need to find the value for 0.99 on the table.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/continuous-z-score-tables?u=76281980&t=98)** Along the right, you'll look for 0.9 and at the top of the table, you'll move along to the right until you find 0.09. Where that row and that column intersect, we find 0.8389. What does that number mean? Well, a Z-score table value of 0.8389 tells us that for a Z-score of 0.99, which is really close to 1.0, 83.89% of all possible outcomes are smaller, and 16.11% of all possible outcomes are bigger. If you look at the area under the curve on either side of the red line, that seems to make sense. Also, remember, 68% of outcomes are between negative one and positive one standard deviations. 32% are outside of that range, which means we should expect about 16% of outcomes to be bigger on the positive side of the curve. Let's see how [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] can be useful in giving us those Z-score table values. So here you can see that we have data for NBA player heights, and as it says at the top, we're going to assume that NBA player heights are normally distributed. The first thing that we want to do is we want to
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/continuous-z-score-tables?u=76281980&t=192)** find our Z-scores. And notice I've given you the mean and the standard deviation. So what we can do at this point, again, we are assuming that our NBA player heights are normally distributed, is we're going to find the Z-score for each data point. So we're going to take the data point minus our mean, and we're going to be using this over and over again. So I want to put my dollar signs on that, close our parentheses, and we're going to divide by the standard deviation, which again we're going to be using over and over again. So I'll put my dollar signs on that as well. So you can see right here that this NBA player, player 1317, has a Z-score of 1.52. And I'm going to move our red line here to about 1.52 and you can see that right about here. And the next thing we want to do is we want to use the Z chart table values, but instead of going to the table, we can have Excel do the work for us. We can have Excel find the table value for us. So what we're going to do is we're going to type in norm and then we're going to look for NORM.S.DIST. We're going to click on this and this is going to say to us, I can find you the Z chart value. I just need to know the Z-score that you're looking for. So we're looking for 1.520. And do we want this to be cumulative?
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/continuous-z-score-tables?u=76281980&t=286)** Yes, we want this to be part of the cumulative distribution function. So we're going to click true. We're going to close this up. And what you can see is that the Z-score value for 1.520 is 0.9357, which means that 93.57% of NBA players are shorter than this player according to the normal distribution. And so let's go over here and you can see it looks like that's a pretty good guess here from our normal distribution curve, that about 93% of the area under this blue curve is to the left of that red line. And that would mean that one minus 93.57%,
>
> **[5:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/continuous-z-score-tables?u=76281980&t=337)** so about 6.43% of the area under the curve is to the right of that value. And we can do this for all players. So when we go over here to, let's say, this player, their Z-score is about negative 0.63. So I'm going to move this over here to about here. And once again, we are expecting about 26% of the area under the curve to be to the left. 26.13% of NBA players are shorter than this player. And about 73.87% of NBA players are expected to be taller than this player. All right folks, that's pretty much it. As you can see, Excel does a lot of the work for you. It can find the Z-score table value, and you can immediately figure out where you are on this normal curve.
