#### [Sample considerations](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/sample-considerations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/sample-considerations?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Inference. What does that mean? Well, it means that we reach a conclusion. A conclusion based on evidence. This course is largely about statistical inference, which means that in this [[Statistics]] course, we'll make some evidence-based conclusions. So what will we use as evidence? Samples. Let me explain. Suppose we want to know the average height of females in a certain city, well, that poses some problems. Which problems? Well, we don't have the time nor the money to measure everyone's height, and most people probably aren't willing to let us measure their height. And this is where samples come in. By taking a small group, a sample of women and getting their heights, we can use this as evidence to draw some conclusions about the average height of women in the city. That said, there's some things to consider. First, what's the sample size? In a city of about 25,000 women, how many women's heights would you need? 100 women, 1000, 10,000? Needing only 100 women's heights would be great. A small sample size is cheaper and faster to collect, but small sample sizes often have large margins of error. Remember, we want solid evidence to draw our conclusions. Second, what's your selection process? Suppose you said 250 women's heights
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/sample-considerations?u=76281980&t=95)** would be a good sample size. Well, how will you choose the 250 women? Will you choose women that go to the doctor's [[Microsoft Office|office]], women leaving the grocery store? Maybe you can try phone surveys or perhaps use a paid survey. We pay women to get their heights measured. I'm not sure any of those is a viable idea. And even if they were, each of those selection options is flawed. Which brings us to our third issue, bias. There are many ways samples can be biased. There's selection bias. If we select women at the doctor's office, we may be measuring women that are healthier or sicker or perhaps more wealthy than most other people. Phone surveys are biased toward people that answer their phones, paid surveys, bring in people that want money. And then there's polling bias. If we survey people outside the grocery store, perhaps our pollster is more likely to ask kinder looking women, more attractive women, or women that are similar to our pollster. A fourth issue to consider, methodology. How are we measuring? Are we using a ruler, a tape measure, a stadiometer? Are we letting people keep their shoes on? Is our pollster reminding people to stand up straight? In other words, are we being very careful or are we a bit sloppy? In essence, our goal is to collect the best sample possible because we want solid evidence
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/sample-considerations?u=76281980&t=189)** so we can make an informed conclusion. The best possible sample is what statisticians call the simple random sample. But gathering a simple random sample is not that simple. Actually, it's quite difficult. Why? Well, a simple random sample must exhibit two key characteristics. The sample must be unbiased, and the data points must be independent. We already discussed bias. Making sure every person selected is actually randomly selected. That is very difficult. Where we collect data, when and how we collect data, who's collecting the data? There are so many ways bias can creep into our sample. And then there's independence. The data in the sample must exhibit independence. What does this mean? It means that the selection of one participant must not influence the selection of other participants. Suppose we pay women to participate in our study. Is it possible a person that volunteers tells their friends about this paid opportunity? If so, now we have numerous people from the same friend group. #### [The central limit theorem](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/the-central-limit-theorem?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/the-central-limit-theorem?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The central limit theorem, it's a rather simple concept that is sort of intuitive, but with some interesting and helpful twists. We've already started to discover that when we take a sample, the larger our sample size, the more confident we are in our sample. Confident about what? Confident that this one sample reflects the entire population. But the central limit theorem takes this a bit further. The central limit theorem goes on to tell us that the more samples we take, the closer the average of our sample means will get to the actual population mean. So, if we take one sample with a reasonable sample size, we have some evidence. If we take lots of samples, the evidence starts to point us closer and closer to the truth. But the central limit theorem actually tells us a bit more. It says that as we start to take many more samples, dozens of samples, hundreds of samples, even thousands of samples, the sample means, if we plotted them as a histogram, they would begin to look more and more like a normal distribution. But it goes one step further. If we take hundreds of random samples with a small sample size of four, the distribution might look like this, and that's if we use a sample size of only four data points. But look what happens when we take hundreds
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/the-central-limit-theorem?u=76281980&t=94)** of random samples with a sample size of 50. The distribution starts to look like this. The curve is getting taller and more narrow, which means a larger sample size gives us a smaller standard deviation. Let's actually see this inion by using [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]]. Okay, so what are we looking at here? What you have been given is a population of data, 950 students, and you have a course grade for 950 students. We also know that the max course grade among these 950 students is 999 points, and the lowest course grade among these 950 students is 347. Let's go ahead and figure out what the population mean is of these scores. So, we're going to do an average of everything in column D. So, we can see that the average course grade is 706.2. The next thing we we want to do is we want to see what types of course grades we have in this particular class. So, we're going to build a frequency table. And we're going to use the bins that we have here from F6 down to right there, about F20. Close that up,

We're going to grab our data from our exam scores, which they start here at D2, and it goes all the way down to, we know there's 950 exam scores and we have a label up there, so it's going to go down to D951. And because we're going to be using this over and over again, I'm going to lock these cells in. Next thing we need to do is gather one of those numbers at random. So, we're going to use the RANDBETWEEN
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/the-central-limit-theorem?u=76281980&t=285)** and we want to gather an exam score between the first exam score and the 950th exam score. And there we go, we have one of our exam scores. Now, we want to do this, we want to gather some big samples today. So, let's go ahead and we're going to copy this all the way over to the right. So, we'll have a sample of 36 different readings. We can also copy this down, and look what we just did. We now have 10 samples, and each sample has up to 36 course grades in them. Now, the next thing I want to do is say, "Well, not all my samples are big." Sometimes we have small samples, sometimes they have medium size, sometimes they're a little bit bigger. So, I'm going to say, "Well, what would happen if we said our sample was only a sample size of four, so n is equal to four, what would be the mean?" So, we're going to use our average, but I only want the average of the first four. Or how about if I want the average of the first 16 scores? Or how about if I want the average of all 36 scores? And what we would expect is that the larger the sample size, the closer we're getting to our population mean,
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/the-central-limit-theorem?u=76281980&t=380)** and it doesn't always happen, but it should happen most of the time. What I'm going to do is I'm going to do this for all of our samples. So, I'm going to copy this down, and the next thing that we want to do is we want to take the average of our averages, the mean of our means. So, we'll do that right here. Say, all right, the average of all 10 of my n equals four means is this. And the average of my n equals 16 means is the average of all of these. And I can copy across again. And the average of all of my n equals 36 samples is this right here. And again, we have this RAN function that's living inside of all these cells. So, every single time we change something, the numbers change. But you can sort of see already how as things are changing, this n equals 36 tends to be the one that's pretty close to our population mean. But let's go ahead and do one thing. The blues, which are n equals four,
>
> **[9:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/the-central-limit-theorem?u=76281980&t=573)** that distribution is always rather low and rather wide. But as we start to get the larger sample size, the orange n equals 16, that gets a little bit more narrow and a little bit taller. And by the time we get to n equals 36, we have a very narrow curve with a very tall center. And again, we can sort of see this over and over again.
>
> **[10:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/the-central-limit-theorem?u=76281980&t=609)** And there you go, the central limit in action. The larger our sample size gets, the smaller our standard deviation and the taller our curve becomes.

#### [t-statistic vs. z-statistic](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/t-statistic-vs-z-statistic?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/t-statistic-vs-z-statistic?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] By now we're pretty well acquainted with the normal distribution. We know that many populations are normally distributed. So if we have a sample with a large sample size, and if we know the population standard deviation, we can feel comfortable using the normal distribution and finding z-scores. But most of the time, we don't have the resources to gather a sufficiently large sample and rarely do we know the population standard deviation. Small sample sizes and no access to the population standard deviation, those are two [[Forms]] of uncertainty. If we gather 100 samples, and each sample has a sample size of only five data points, we start to run into a problem. The distribution of the sample means won't exactly look like the normal distribution. The distribution curve would be a bit more flat and the tails would be a bit more fat. This is not the normal distribution. In other words, the z-distribution won't work, which is why we need something called the t-distribution. The z-distribution and the t-distribution, how are they similar? Both are bell-shaped distributions, both are symmetrical around the mean, and in both cases, the area under the curve is equal to one. So how are they different? Well, the t-distribution isn't one curve,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/t-statistic-vs-z-statistic?u=76281980&t=95)** but rather a series of curves. Each curve is representative of the distribution for different sample sizes. The smaller the sample size, the flatter the curve. But as the sample size increases, our t-distribution curves begin to look more and more like our traditional bell-shaped normal distribution curve. So while a z-distribution has only one curve, and thus only one table for z-scores, since we have multiple d-distribution curves, each curve must have its own table for t-scores. Here's an example of one of those tables. It looks similar to the z-table, but you'll notice something along the left side, a column labeled as df. This stands for degrees of freedom. What are degrees of freedom? The easy answer is that degrees of freedom is just our sample size minus one. Also referred to as n-1. N is the amount of data points in our sample. So for a sample size of five, we have four degrees of freedom. For a sample size of 10, we have 9 degrees of freedom. Yes, there's a more complex explanation for degrees of freedom, but let's leave that for another day. Nonetheless, even once we figure out that degrees of freedom is just n-1, this chart still doesn't look like a whole lot of fun. But, thankfully, we have [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]]. Let's head over to Excel to see the t-statistic in action.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/t-statistic-vs-z-statistic?u=76281980&t=192)** Let's first recap. So what I've given you here are some course grades. And I've already figured out for you the population mean for these course grades, and I've given you the standard deviation for this entire population as well. So what we're going to do, we have the population mean, we have the population standard deviation. So that means that using a z-score here is perfectly acceptable. First thing we want to do is how do you figure out what the z-score is for each particular grade? We can see the formula right over here. Z is equal to the data point, 92, minus our population mean, which is 70.8, and that is divided by our population standard deviation. So let's go ahead and plug that in over here. All right, so we now know that the z-score for a course grade of 92 with a population mean of 70.8 and a standard deviation of 16.4, the z-score is 1.2939. Now, where does that sit on the distribution curve? Well, for this we, can use a formula, NORM.S.DIST. And it just says, "Tell me what the z-score is," and we want to type in true here, "Tell me what the z-score is, and I will tell you what the [[Probability]] of that score is." So you can see right here, this score is 90.22% higher than all the other scores in the distribution.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/t-statistic-vs-z-statistic?u=76281980&t=286)** And if we want, because we can, well, first thing that we want to do is we want to make sure that if we're going to copy this down, that we lock in our population mean, which we'll be using over and over again. And the same thing with our population standard deviation. So now that those are locked in, this will continue to change. As the z-score changes, we can now copy this down. We now have z-scores for every grade in the course. Now, the next thing we want to do is I want to refresh you on how do you find z-scores and how do you go from percentage to a z-score and back and forth. And what you'll notice here is I just copied this particular z-score. And we're going to use this over and over again since we know the probability of that z-score is .9022. One thing that we can do is once somebody gives us a z-score, we can do a NORM.S.DIST, and we provide the z-score, and we just saw this a moment ago, and that gave us the percentage. But you can go the other way as well. What you can do is you can use the NORM.S.INV, I-N-V, and provide it a probability, and this will give you the associated z-score. The other thing I want to show you is this NORM.DIST function all by itself. And what this does is it finds the probability of a certain score. Let's go ahead and type that in.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/t-statistic-vs-z-statistic?u=76281980&t=378)** NORM.DIST. And you'll notice here, what this is asking us for is which data point do you want to find your z-score for? So the first thing that we're going to do is we're going to go ahead and say, "Well, for a score of 92, in a population that has a mean of 70.8 and a standard deviation of 16.4, on a cumulative distribution, that score right there would be in the 90.22 probability. Again, if we want to do it the other way, let's go ahead and do NORM.INV. In this case, someone says, "Well, give us the probability, tell us what the standard population mean is for this particular population, give us the standard deviation for the population, and we can tell you what the exam score is going to be." The other thing that you can do that's kind of interesting with these z-scores is you can use this NORM-INV function. And if somebody said to you, "If I wanted to be in the 90th percentile of the class, if I wanted to be higher than 90% of the scores in the class, and the class average was 70.8 and the standard deviation was 16.4,
>
> **[7:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/t-statistic-vs-z-statistic?u=76281980&t=476)** a student that has a 91.8% on the exam is in the 90th percentile." And that makes sense because this student right here with a 92% is a little bit above that. All right, so we've now refreshed ourselves on the z-score going from z-scores to percentage and from percentage to z-scores and finding these magic numbers that put us at different places on the distribution. Let's go ahead and see what we can do with the t-statistic. So what you'll notice here is I've given you one single random sample. So this is what happens with the t-statistic. You're given one sample, it's usually a small sample, and we don't have access to the population standard deviation. So the first thing that I need to do is, "Well, what's my sample size?" So I'm just going to count up how many values I have here. And our sample size is 20. We also now know that our degrees of freedom, remember, our degrees of freedom is our sample size minus one. So let's go ahead and take care of that right now. It's our sample size minus one. So we have 19 degrees of freedom. The sample of my mean is just the average of the 20 data points that we have here. And we also want to know the standard deviation for our sample. So we click in standard deviation. So STDEV.S
>
> **[9:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/t-statistic-vs-z-statistic?u=76281980&t=569)** 'cause this is just for a sample, not for a population. And we're going to take everything here. And so now we know that the sample for our standard deviation is 17.36. At this point, we are now ready to start utilizing our t-distribution. And you'll notice here to the right, I've given you a picture of what the t-distributions might look like. So first thing that we want to do, we'll use this formula, T.INV. And what this is going to do is it's going to allow us to figure out where on our distribution a certain percentage is going to be. In this case, I chose the percentage of 0.975. And the next thing we need to do is provide it the degrees of freedom. Because, remember, the larger the sample size, the larger our degrees of freedom, the larger our degrees of freedom, the taller our curve that were associated with it. So we have 19 degrees of freedom. And what does this mean? Well, what it's telling us is that, I'm going to use this orange bar right here, it just gave us a t-score of 2.093. So it's right about here. And what this is telling us is that this is where 97.5% of our distribution is to the left of the orange bar and 2.5% of our distribution
>
> **[11:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/t-statistic-vs-z-statistic?u=76281980&t=663)** is to the right of the orange bar. So now that has changed this. But, again, we're using 19 degrees of freedom, so let's go ahead and put that back. All right, so let's use a number of different functions to find our way around on this t-distribution. First one we're going to do is the T.DIST function. And what this is asking us for is, "Well, what's the t-score that you're looking at here?" Well, we're looking at 2.093. How many degrees of freedom do you have? We have 19 degrees of freedom. And we want a cumulative distribution. And so when you calculate using this formula right here, it's a one-tail test and it's the amount to the left of the t-score. So this is telling us when we've created this 2.09 t-score, 97.5% of the distribution is to the left. If we wanted to do it the other way around, well, there's a function for that as well. ### 10. Confidence Intervals

#### [Introduction to confidence intervals](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/introduction-to-confidence-intervals?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/introduction-to-confidence-intervals?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Host] Confidence intervals are statistical tools. They are both very helpful and very efficient. Depending on the situation, confidence intervals allow us to guess at either the true average or the true proportion for an entire population. And here's the incredible thing. They can do it with the use of only one single sample. So how is this done? Well, a confidence interval might provide us with a result that looks like this. We are 95% confident that the average adult in the United States sleeps between five and seven hours per night. When you read that, I want you to remember with one simple random sample, this statistician created an interval, an interval that stretches from a lower limit to an upper limit. In this case, it's stretched from five to seven hours. Perhaps even more interesting, though, the statistician assigned a level of confidence to that interval. In this example, the statistician that created the interval is 95% certain that for US adults, the actual sleep average for the entire US population is between five and seven hours of sleep. Think about how incredibly powerful this is. One single random sample that allows us to be 95% confident that our interval contains the real population mean.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/introduction-to-confidence-intervals?u=76281980&t=95)** That's an incredibly efficient use of resources. Let's take a moment to discuss 95% confidence level. What does that mean? Well, suppose the actual average sleep for the US population is 6.25 hours. And then suppose we took 20 individual random samples and for each of those 20 samples, we created a 95% confidence interval. We would now have 20 different 95% confidence intervals. Well, we would expect 95% or 19 of those confidence intervals to contain the real population mean of 6.25 hours and we'd expect 5% or one confidence interval to not contain the real population mean. As you can see, sample number six does not contain the real population mean of 6.25 hours. As for the basic formulas for confidence intervals, they usually look like this. First, we use the average provided by our sample. We then need a critical value for the confidence level we're looking for. Often this will be a Z score, but in some cases it could be a T score. Finally, on the right of our formula, we also see a need for a sampling error, a sampling error that is directly related to our sample size. And remember, we need two values.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/introduction-to-confidence-intervals?u=76281980&t=188)** The upper limit of our confidence interval, the sample average plus the Z value times the sampling error, and the lower limit of our confidence interval sample average minus the Z value times the sampling error. In these formulas, I assumed we'd need a Z-score. Then again, we'll be using [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]]. So Excel will likely be doing most of the calculations for us. All right, now we know what confidence intervals are. In our next videos, we'll create some confidence intervals for both proportions and means using Excel.

#### [Creating confidence intervals in Excel: Proportions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/creating-confidence-intervals-in-excel-proportions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/creating-confidence-intervals-in-excel-proportions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Before we jump to [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] to create confidence intervals for proportions, let's take a look at the formula and the elements of the formula that we'll use to create the upper limit and the lower limit of our confidence intervals. The formula for the upper limit is p-hat plus a z-value times the expression under the square root symbol, which is actually our sampling error. What do the letters mean in the formula? P-hat is the proportion of our random sample, n is the size of our random sample, and z will be the z-score associated to our desired level of confidence. The formula for the lower limit is p-hat minus a z-value times the standard deviation, or standard error, in this case. Let's do a quick example. Suppose for a certain city, we want to know the proportion of homes that had dogs. First, let's look at p-hat. Remember, we're taking one simple random sample to create our confidence interval. P-hat will be the proportion of homes with at least one dog in our single random sample. 40 out of 100 homes have dogs. We would say our p-hat for this sample is 0.40. Now we need the standard deviation, but since we don't know the standard deviation for our entire population, we can use this formula, the formula for standard error, which is also called our sampling error. The standard error is a good approximation of the population's standard deviation.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/creating-confidence-intervals-in-excel-proportions?u=76281980&t=95)** Here is the calculation for the standard error. We know p-hat is 0.40 and we know our sample contained 100 homes. So n is equal to 100. This gives us a standard error of about 0.049. Let's round that to 0.05. Finally, we need our z-score. To find this, we'll first want to remember the empirical rule. The empirical rule states that for a normal distribution, we would expect about 68% of our samples to fall within one standard deviation of the true population proportion. We would expect about 95% of our samples to fall within two standard deviations of our population proportion, and we'd expect about 99.7% of our samples to fall within three standard deviations of our population proportion. Remember, z-score is a measure of the number of standard deviations. This is why for a 95% confidence interval, we should expect our z-score to be very close to 2.0. To be more exact, it's closer to 1.96, but we'll discuss that when we jump to Excel. For now, let's just use 2.0. So let's calculate our upper and lower limits. In this example, p-hat is 0.40, our z-score is 2.0, and our standard error is 0.05. So our upper limit is 0.50, and our lower limit is 0.30.

>
> **[7:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/creating-confidence-intervals-in-excel-proportions?u=76281980&t=473)** that we have now found this red line over here, 'cause it's excluding the 2.5% on this end. And then we'll know that that's also going to be the same on the other end over here. So the number that we're actually looking for for our z-score is 1.96, which is what we have over here. And I'm going to copy that down. The next thing we're looking for is our standard error. And if you remember, the formula for our standard error is going to be the square root of p-hat times one minus p-hat all over our n. And this is the formula I've put in here. The square root of our p-hat, P8, times 1 minus P8, and all over our sample size, which is n. And what I've done here is I've locked that in, 'cause we're always going to be using the same sample size so I can copy this down as well. So at this point, we have our p-hat, which is what we need for our upper limit. We have our z and we have our standard error, and that's going to allow us to calculate our upper and lower limit. So let's go ahead and do that. Our lower limit is going to be equal to our p-hat. No, wrong one. Our upper limit, our lower limit, rather, is going to be equal to our p-hat minus our z-score, times our standard error. That's our lower limit, and our upper limit is going to be p-hat plus our z-score times our standard error.
>
> **[9:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/creating-confidence-intervals-in-excel-proportions?u=76281980&t=571)** And there we go. Now what I've also done is I've put in a test in here to see whether or not this actually contains our population proportion. So remember, our population proportion is 54%. We would expect the lower limit number to be smaller than 54%, and we'd expect our upper limit lumber to be bigger than 54%. And so we now have a test, and it looks like this one works. I'm going to copy these down, and then I'm going to copy down the tests as well. And what we would expect is that 95%, or 19 out of 20, would actually contain our population proportion, but it doesn't seem to be working out very well right here. And we'll take a number of other samples every time I change this. This one, 95%. This one, oh, we had two, so it's about 90%. This one, oh, we had quite a few. And you might be saying, well, what's going on here? Well, what the problem could be is that our distribution is not normally distributed as we would expect, because one thing is, our sample size is very small. And so as a result, that could be problematic, which, of course, is something that we want to think about. Larger sample size are more helpful. So let's go ahead and do this with a sample size of 50. So I've already put the formulas in here. I've indexed column A, I've indexed down here.
>
> **[11:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/creating-confidence-intervals-in-excel-proportions?u=76281980&t=667)** And what I'm going to do is I'm actually going to grab a sample of 50 different votes all in one single sample. So copy right, copy down. These 50 votes are all part of a single sample. So our sample size is 50. Here's our p-hat, it's the sum of all of these divided by our sample size of 50. Our z-value is the same, 'cause we still want a 95% confidence interval. And our standard error, well, in this case it's pretty much the same. It's the square root of p-hat, times one minus this p-hat, and all over our sample size of 50. And so our standard error is this. It's a smaller standard error, so that means our lower and upper control limits are going to be a little bit tighter. But in this case here, because our sample size is bigger, we're feeling a little bit more confident about our confidence interval overall. And you'll notice that pretty much no matter what I do, in most cases, it's actually collecting a confidence interval that does contain my actual population proportion. So we've seen a number of different things here. We've seen how to create confidence intervals for proportions, and we've also seen the impact of utilizing a larger sample size. Smaller sample sizes may sometimes not adhere
>
> **[12:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/creating-confidence-intervals-in-excel-proportions?u=76281980&t=761)** to the normal distribution. So having that larger sample size can be very helpful.

Here are the formulas for the upper and lower limits. In the formula's X-bar is the mean of our sample, 36 pounds. And let's say we're going to create a 95% confidence interval. We've used that in a previous video. We know that the critical value, the Z-score, is 1.96. So now we need to find the standard error. The formula for the standard error is sigma, our population standard deviation, 15, divided by the square root of N, the sample size, 25. The square root of 25 is five, so our standard error is three. This means the upper and lower control limits for our 95% confidence interval would be 36, our mean, plus and minus 1.96, the Z-score, times three, the standard error. ### 11. Hypothesis Tests

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [How to test a hypothesis](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/how-to-test-a-hypothesis?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/how-to-test-a-hypothesis?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In a trial, lawyers use evidence in an attempt to prove a person is either guilty or not guilty. So far in [[Statistics]], we've seen evidence. Samples are our evidence. But when we have a belief, how do we put that belief on trial? How do we use our evidence to reach a sound conclusion? We use hypothesis tests. Hypothesis tests is like a trial. It is a formal process for testing whether we have sufficient evidence to draw a conclusion. For example, let's say the National Dog Association says that the average US dog weighs 40 pounds. You take a sample of 25 dogs in your town and find that the average weight of those dogs is only 35 pounds. Perhaps you begin to doubt the National Dog Association's published average. The question is, do you have enough evidence to say that the average US dog probably weighs less than 40 pounds? Suppose you're the statistics lawyer. You've been given this one sample as your evidence. What concerns you about the sample provided? First, the stated average was 40 pounds. The sample mean was 35 pounds. Is this significant enough to make me doubt the stated average? Next, the sample only had a sample size of 25; that's a small sample size to represent the whole country.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/how-to-test-a-hypothesis?u=76281980&t=94)** Can I trust the sample? Also, what's the standard deviation of the sample? Maybe a couple of tiny dogs in the sample had a big impact on this particular sample. Well, hypothesis testing takes all of these issues into account. The calculations utilize sample averages, sample size, standard deviation, and the normal distribution. Just like a trial, there is a process that must be followed though. In the next video, we will look at the five-step process that must be followed to properly perform a hypothesis test.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Statistics]] (2)
> **CLI Commands:** find (1), make (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)
> **Analogies:** for example (1), just like (1)
> **Cross-References:** in the next (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Five steps of hypothesis testing](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/five-steps-of-hypothesis-testing?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/five-steps-of-hypothesis-testing?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] [[Statistics]] help us draw sound conclusions, conclusions that are based on evidence. To do this properly, though, we need to have a fair trial. In statistics, this trial is called a hypothesis test. And to properly perform a hypothesis test, we need to follow five steps. Let's discover the five steps by performing a simple hypothesis test. Suppose the College Basketball Association states that the average height of a male college basketball player is 195 centimeters. A random sample is taken with a sample size of 25. The sample mean for the sample is 201 centimeters. We begin to doubt the College Basketball Association's stated average. This looks like a good opportunity for a hypothesis test. Hypothesis testing is a five-step process. In an effort to keep you focused on each step, we're going to keep the math very simple. So let's get started. In our first step, we need to set up our hypothesis. There will typically be two hypotheses. H0 are null hypothesis. The null hypothesis is what is presently accepted as fact. Our other hypothesis is H sub a. This is our alternative hypothesis, which states something contradictory to our null hypothesis. So our H0 says that mu is equal to 195 centimeters. Why?
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/five-steps-of-hypothesis-testing?u=76281980&t=95)** Because presently it's believed the average player is 195 centimeters tall. But we believe it might be wrong. We think the average player might be taller than 195 centimeters. So our alternative hypothesis, H sub a, says mu is greater than 195. Our next step is to state a significance level, which we will call alpha. This is how we'll judge our trial. Essentially, this sets a threshold for our test. How far from the expected average does our sample average need to be for us to reject the null hypothesis? In many cases, statisticians like to utilize a significance level of 5%. If there is less than a 5% chance, a random sample of 25 players could have an average of 201 centimeters, less than a 1 in 20 chance, then we will reject the null hypothesis. By the way, sometimes people want to use a smaller alpha, perhaps 1%, but we'll use 5% for this example. Next, we need to identify the test statistic. What will we use to measure our sample to the expected outcome? We'll assume the player heights are normally distributed. So, how likely is it that we get a random sample of 25 that has an X-bar of 201 centimeters? We also have the expected population mean
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/five-steps-of-hypothesis-testing?u=76281980&t=192)** and standard deviation. For this, we can use a Z-score. This will be our test statistic. So now we can calculate our Z-score. Remember, X-bar is 201, the population mean is 195, the standard deviation for the population is 12, and our sample size is 25. Therefore, our Z-score is 2.50. Step four, our P-value. We have our null hypothesis, our significance level, and our test statistic. Next, we need to find the P-value for that test statistic. The P-value is the [[Probability]] that this outcome could occur by chance. So we go to our Z-score table. We know our Z-score is 2.50, which gives us a value of 0.9938. Our X-bar is greater than 99.38% of expected values, which means our P-value is 0.0062. Wow, that outcome is not very likely at all. Step five, it's time to compare our P-value to the fixed significance level that we established. We set our alpha as 0.05 or 5%. This is what it looks like on our normal distribution. Anything to the right of that line means we reject our null hypothesis.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/five-steps-of-hypothesis-testing?u=76281980&t=286)** Anything to the left of that line means we do not reject our null hypothesis. Our P-value is 0.0062. There is only a 0.62% chance. This sample occurred by chance, which means it is to the right of our threshold. Therefore, we must reject our null hypothesis. So what does that mean? There are only two possible outcomes in a hypothesis test: reject the null hypothesis or do not reject the null hypothesis. Notice, do not reject the null hypothesis doesn't mean that we accept the alternative hypothesis. Reject simply means we contradicted the null hypothesis. Do not reject means that we could not contradict the null hypothesis. It's sort of like saying a person on trial is either guilty or not guilty. Guilty means the evidence is there to convict. Not guilty means that there was a lack of evidence. Not guilty does not necessarily mean the jury believed the person was innocent. They just lacked the evidence to prove guilt. Hypothesis testing is an extremely important part of the world of statistics and every other field that leans on statistics for assistance.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

> **Definitions:** means that (2), is called (1), is a  (1), is an  (1)

#### [One-tailed vs. two-tailed tests](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/one-tailed-vs-two-tailed-tests?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/one-tailed-vs-two-tailed-tests?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When we perform hypothesis tests, we're looking for outcomes that are out of the ordinary. Statistically, what does that mean? Well, if we look at our normal distribution curve, it means that we're looking for outcomes that are on the fringes, outcomes that are in the tail sections of our distribution. We have two tails, one on the right and one on the left. Sometimes we're testing for outcomes that are far too low. They are on the left side. Sometimes we're testing for outcomes that are far too high. Those are on the right side. And sometimes we're looking for outcomes that are generally far away from the mean. So we're looking for outcomes either very far to the right or very far to the left. This is why we have three types of tests. One-tail test to the left, one-tail test to the right, and the two-tail test. Here's a simple example. Let's suppose a research company develops new medicines to help people with the common cold. This is very much a trial and error process. Some medicines are successful, they help people get better faster than normal, but then there are some experimental pills that seem to actually prolong the illness. And some of the experimental pills don't seem to have much of an impact at all. These three scenarios each require a different type of hypothesis test. Here's the normal curve. Let's say the average adult has the common cold for eight days. That will be the center of our curve, the mean duration of the cold. The normal curve is a distribution illustrating
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/one-tailed-vs-two-tailed-tests?u=76281980&t=94)** all the possible common cold durations. While most samples of patients will have a sample mean that is within the expected outcomes, some samples may be sick much longer than expected, and some samples may be sick much shorter than expected. That said, if we're testing a medicine that is believed to actually help people recover more quickly than normal, we'll develop a test group, our sample, and we'll track the average time it took this group to recover. For this sample, we're hoping to get an outcome on the far left side of the curve. That would tell us that this outcome is statistically unlikely. This would give us evidence that the medicine helped people recover more quickly. This is a one-tail test, a left-tail test. How about if someone thinks our medicine is actually not helpful? They think it weakens people. It makes them sicker for a longer period of time. Again, we need a one-tail test, but here we need a right-tail test. We'll sample a group of people, measure the mean recovery time for the group. If we find this group has a statistically unlikely recovery time, one that is very long, very far to the right, then we will have evidence the medicine is increasing the expected recovery time. Finally, if we're testing a medicine but don't really know if it's having any impact, we may want to perform a two-tail test. We'll take a sample of patients, measure the mean recovery time,
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/one-tailed-vs-two-tailed-tests?u=76281980&t=187)** and we'll see if we get a statistically unlikely outcome in either direction. Maybe it's on the far right, maybe it's on the far left. In either case, we'll want to investigate further. As you begin to perform hypothesis tests, think about what you're looking for, unlikely scenarios on the left, the right, or perhaps both.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)
> **CLI Commands:** find (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Hypothesis test exercise: Proportions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/hypothesis-test-exercise-proportions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/hypothesis-test-exercise-proportions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this video, we'll be looking at hypothesis tests for proportions. We'll compare a sample proportion to an expected outcome. In this scenario, we'll investigate whether an upcoming election is still undecided based on a recent poll of likely voters. We will, of course, use [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] to perform our hypothesis test. But before we head over to Excel, let me show you a formula we'll utilize for this hypothesis test. > **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/hypothesis-test-exercise-proportions?u=76281980&t=95)** H naught says, look, the election hasn't taken place, nobody's won, everybody's equal up until this point. That is this right here, 1.644. That represents the red line. If the number is smaller than 1.64,
>
> **[7:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/hypothesis-test-exercise-proportions?u=76281980&t=458)** that means that we cannot reject the null hypothesis. But in this case, we got a Z-score of 1.88. That's to the right. That means that we're in the rejection zone, that we are really doubting whether this null hypothesis is actually true. All right, so now you know how to do hypothesis tests for proportions, good luck.

### 12. Comparing Two Populations

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Explanation of two population comparisons](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/explanation-of-two-population-comparisons?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/explanation-of-two-population-comparisons?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Up until now, we've tested one population by using one sample, and then testing that one sample versus a statement or belief. But what if we have two groups: for example, think about asking and answering practical questions like this: which is more likely to reduce the chance of a heart attack: aspirin or a placebo? Which group is more likely to own their own company, women in their 20s or men in their 20s? Notice we keep using the words, "More likely," in each scenario. What makes all these questions similar is that each situation can be analyzed by comparing two independent random samples, one from each population. For example, we can compare the proportion of aspirin takers that experienced a heart attack versus the proportion of people that took a placebo and experienced a heart attack. We can do this by using hypothesis testing to compare the difference between the proportions for each of our independent samples. But, this doesn't just work for proportions. We can also analyze two groups that have different means. Consider this situation. 200 males in their 30s are randomly assigned to two groups for a period of three months. One group of males is required to exercise two hours per day, but they're allowed to eat whatever they want. The other group of males are not required to exercise,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/explanation-of-two-population-comparisons?u=76281980&t=95)** but they must adhere to a very strict diet. The males on the strict diet, they lose an average of four pounds more during the three-month period versus the individuals that are required to exercise daily. Is diet a more effective weight loss technique versus daily exercise for males in their 30s, or, was this the result of chance? Sure, the data sounds convincing, but perhaps the weight loss program was not the differentiator. Maybe it was genetics. Perhaps one of the groups had more men genetically inclined to lose more weight. For these men, it didn't matter which group they were in. In this chapter, we'll use [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] to help us compare two populations by performing hypothesis tests on two independent samples, and we'll do it for both proportions and means.

Well, when we don't have access to the population standard deviation, we know we can't use the Z-score, we need to use our T-score. So we're going to get to that in just a moment. But let's look at this particular scenario. We have two fitness counselors, and each of them believes they developed a method for people to lose weight. One of the fitness counselors has developed a program with a very strict diet, but that diet does not require these people to exercise. The other person, they developed an exercise routine. So they said, look, we're going to have you do this daily exercise, but you can eat whatever you'd like. So they create two groups, two small groups and after six months, they measure the results. And the counselor that developed the diet method, they believed that their method is a little bit better. And you'll notice right here, the diet average was 8.8 with a small sample size of 16 people that participated for the six months. And the exercise group averaged 6.2 pounds lost. Their group was a little bit smaller, about 14 for the six months.
>
> **[10:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/comparing-two-populations-means?u=76281980&t=640)** So the question is, does this support the counselor believing that their diet method is better? So let's begin. Step number one, our null hypothesis. We're saying these two groups are the same. Mu one minus Mu two is zero. And of course, the counselor that developed a diet, they say, no, I believe the diet is more effective. So I believe that Mu one, the diet group, minus Mu two, should actually be greater than zero. It looks like a right tail test again, to me. Our level of significance is going to be 5%. We already have our X bar one and our sample size for group number one. The standard deviation of our sample for our diet group is 5.43. And the variance is just that number squared. And then we can do the standard deviation of our sample for our exercise group, and it comes out to be 4.54. Now, if you thought we had some ugly formulas before, well, it's just going to get a little bit uglier right here. So our standard error is our S1 squared, the over N1 plus our S2 squared over N2, and we take the square root of that and happily, I've already input those formulas for you. I found the standard error, which is going to be the bottom, the denominator of our formula.
>
> **[12:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/comparing-two-populations-means?u=76281980&t=732)** We already have X1 and X2 So when we are calculating our T statistic, our T statistic is going to be X bar one minus X bar two. The difference between the two groups divided by our standard error, remember, M one minus Mu two should be zero because that's our null hypothesis. So our T statistic is 1.43, but we're not done. Remember, when we're using the T statistic, we often need to know our degrees of freedom. Here is how you calculate your degrees of freedom. Yes, that really horrible ugly formula right there. I have already done the calculation here for the numerator. I've done a calculation for the denominator. And so all we need to do to find our degrees of freedom is divide the 11.01 by our 0.39 and look at that, 27.96 degrees of freedom. And usually they tell you that you're supposed to round down. In this particular case, I'm going to round up and I'll tell you why in just a little bit, here in just a moment. So now it is time for our step four, which is the P value. Our P value, just going to have to find this, use our T.dist with our T-score, T-score of 1.43, our degrees of freedom.
>
> **[13:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/comparing-two-populations-means?u=76281980&t=828)** I'm going to use 28. So let's put a whole number in there. You don't want to put a decimal and this is going to be true. And oh, I put an extra parentheses in there. Let's take that out. There we go. This is a right tail test. So this right here is our P value, and it looks like this says do not reject. This is 8% likely, and we're looking for something that is less than 5% likely. Wow, that was a lot of work, right? But thankfully, we have Excel, and Excel is going to do a lot of this work for us. Click up on data, you go over data analysis, you go to a T-test. So we're going to do this for two sampling with unequal variances because we have unequal variances in this case, we click okay. And variable range number one is going to be our diet group. Variable range number two is our exercise group. I did use labels. Our hypothesis mean difference is zero. We think that the groups are the same. We're going to use an alpha of 0.05, and let's put our results right here on this page. We'll put them right there. Alright, let's click okay and see what happens. So what can we see here?
>
> **[15:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/comparing-two-populations-means?u=76281980&t=926)** Looks like a lot of numbers that we've already seen before. Here are our means. Here's our variance. Those all seem to match up well with what we have over here, 29.50, 20.64, our observations, our sample size. And then we have the very important T statistic, which is the number we calculated right here, 1.43, 1.426. And this is a one tail test. And a one tail test would give us a critical P value of 0.08. Everything worked out. So whether you want to do this the long way, the fast way, we know that we can trust this particular process.

#### [Hypothesis test and the F-statistic](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/hypothesis-test-and-the-f-statistic?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/hypothesis-test-and-the-f-statistic?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] So in the previous chapter, we were looking at hypothesis tests for two means and for two proportions. And in the previous video in this particular chapter, I told you that ANOVA allows us to compare three or more groups. The thing I didn't tell you is that ANOVA is actually a hypothesis test for three or more groups. And so what we can see right here is that ANOVA is a hypothesis test for three or more groups, and the null hypothesis is always the same. So if you have three groups, the null hypothesis is saying that all of the groups are equal. So the average of group one, mu one, is equal to mu two, is equal to mu three. The groups are actually equal. The alternative hypothesis of course is saying, "Oh no, they're not equal." But it doesn't mean that they're all different either. The alternative hypothesis is actually saying that at least one of the groups is not equal. Now, when we perform a hypothesis test, eventually we've got to make a comparison of our P values. And how do we do that? Usually we use a Z statistic or a T statistic, and then that brings us to the appropriate P value. Well, congratulations. We've now moved on to an F statistic. The F statistic is what you use in ANOVA.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/hypothesis-test-and-the-f-statistic?u=76281980&t=94)** And just like with the T and the Z statistic, there are going to be critical values and there are good Fs and bad F [[Statistics]]. In this case right here, you can see a high F statistic, usually means a low P value, which usually means we reject null hypothesis. We'll get there. We'll get there. For now, let's begin. Let's say that we are going to compare these three groups. The population mean is five and the groups averages are different. But again, these are three different groups, and each group only has a sample size of three. So these are really small samples. So we're going to ask, are these three groups actually the same or different? So our null hypothesis is that group one, the average of group one, the average of group two, and the average of group three are actually the same. These are just small samples. So let's begin our calculations. And I've done a lot of the work for you already. Some of the stuff we saw in the previous video where we were figuring out our sum of squares within and our sum of squares between, and then we have our total sum of squares. And if you don't remember how to do that, you can go ahead and watch the previous video. But I've done a lot of this for you. And of course, [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] does all this work for you if you'd like. But as we begin to say to ourselves, well, are these groups the same or different? How do we access that F statistic? And the F statistic, you'll see right here,
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/hypothesis-test-and-the-f-statistic?u=76281980&t=187)** is equal to SSB, our sum of squares between, divided by K minus one divided by, so this divided by SSW are sum of squares within, divided by N sub T minus K. Now you might go, "Well wait, where is this K and N sub T? Where are they coming from?" K is the number of group, which is also sometimes called the number of levels. So in this case, we have three different groups. So RK is three. N sub T, that stands for the total number of observations. So in total, how many data points did we have between the three groups? We had three data points for group one, three data points for group two and three data points for group three. So our total observations is nine. And so now we need K minus one. And you might wonder, well what is this K minus one? K minus one, that is the degrees of freedom between the groups. So this, obviously, there's going to be two. K is three minus one, and N sub T minus K, that's the degrees of freedom within. So we have nine total data points, but three groups. So we take away one level of freedom with each one, and that's going to give us six. So this right here is going to be equal to,
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/hypothesis-test-and-the-f-statistic?u=76281980&t=281)** I'll take our numerator first, our numerator is going to be equal to our sum of squares between, which is 24, divided by K, which is three minus one. And so that is our numerator. Our denominator is going to be equal to our sum of squares within, divided by our total data points, which is nine, minus the number of groups that we have, which is three. So we have 12 divided by three. My F statistic is four. But what does that mean? Is that good or is that bad? So we have to go to an F table. And when you start to see degrees of freedom, you're probably saying to yourself, "Oh, this probably means that there are different F statistic tables for each degree of freedom." And you're right, this actually gets pretty complicated. So well, you'll notice, this is an F table with critical values for an alpha of five of 0.05 for 5%. So that means there's actually an F table of critical for every single alpha. And so that's why we're going to choose an alpha of 0.05 'cause it's just easier. And then how do you find your F critical value here? Well, remember our degrees of freedom. These become important because degree of freedom number one is that K minus one. So you go over here on your table along the top
>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/hypothesis-test-and-the-f-statistic?u=76281980&t=379)** and you'll find DF one is two, and then your degree of freedom number two, that's what's going to be along the left side. So degree of freedom one, two, degree of freedom two is six, and that intersection point right there, that is our critical F value, 5.14. And so what does this tell us? Well, it now becomes, very much, like a normal hypothesis test. If our F statistic is larger than the critical value, we reject the null hypothesis. But if our R statistic is smaller than the critical value, we do not reject our null hypothesis. So in this case, we are not going to reject our null hypothesis. Now of course, we can have Excel do a lot of this work for us. So we're going to go over to data, data analysis. We're going to perform our ANOVA single factor. Sum of squares between, sum of squares within,
>
> **[7:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/hypothesis-test-and-the-f-statistic?u=76281980&t=474)** total, all things we've seen before. But hopefully you also say, "Oh, look at that, right there." That is the F value that we calculated. There is the critical F value that we found on our table four and 5.14. The other thing that's helpful here is, they've given us a P value, and that P value is about 7.8%. Remember, we have a 5% alpha, and so this is larger than that. So we do not reject our null hypothesis. So this is another way you can test this. All right, so we've done this for our small little group right here. Let's perform a couple more of these. We'll do this one for a group of, in this case, we're going to take four different companies, four different groups, four different levels, and we're going to have customer satisfaction scores for these four different restaurants. And we're going to perform a hypothesis test. Are these groups, in this case our null hypothesis, should include a fourth company? Are these four groups equal? Mu one is equal to mu two is equal to mu three is equal to mu four. That is my null hypothesis. So let's go ahead and perform single factor ANOVA. Our group here, we'll include those.
>
> **[9:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/hypothesis-test-and-the-f-statistic?u=76281980&t=572)** Labels are in the first row, our alpha is here, and I'm going to put my results. I'll just put 'em, yeah, let's put them right here. We click okay. And what do we have? We now have our data points, our N, our sum, our averages. The averages are a little bit different. And we also have the variance for each group. The sum of squares between groups is 11.84. The sum of squares within the groups is 100. So this is our total sum of squares. But again, what we're really doing is, performing a hypothesis test. And the hypothesis test is telling us that our F, our calculated F, is 0.828.
>
> **[10:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/hypothesis-test-and-the-f-statistic?u=76281980&t=626)** Our critical value is 3.07. So as we've learned, if our calculated F value is smaller than our critical value, we do not reject the null hypothesis. So this is telling us that these four groups here, at least one of them is not equal. So we do not reject the null hypothesis. As a result, we have to say that these groups, there is not enough evidence to say that they are different. Okay, let's move on to mobile service. And one other thing I wanted to point out here, our P value, remember our P value is being compared to 0.05. And what you'll notice is that my P value is much, much higher than my critical value of 0.05. So we can definitely not reject it, the null hypothesis. Let's do one more here. We're going to do this with three companies with mobile service and they have their customer satisfaction scores. So once again, go to data, data analysis, ANOVA single factor. I'm going to choose this as my input range. We have labels again, alpha of 0.05 and my output range. I will put my answers or my table right here, and we click okay. And here we go.
>
> **[12:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/hypothesis-test-and-the-f-statistic?u=76281980&t=721)** We have our sum of squares between, sum of squares within, our total sum of squares, and these numbers are bigger, so that's why these numbers here have gotten a little bit bigger. But again, the really important thing, if we are performing a hypothesis test, our F, and that is going to be my calculated F. This is my critical F in this case here. My F is larger than the critical value. So as a result, we can reject the null hypothesis. Once again, if you're not comfortable, using the critical values, remember, we are going to be looking at our P value versus an alpha of 0.05.
>
> **[12:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/hypothesis-test-and-the-f-statistic?u=76281980&t=774)** So if our outcome is less than 5% likely, in this case, it's less than one half of a percent likely, in this case, we definitely want to reject the null hypothesis. All right, so you've done it. You've gotten through ANOVA. You know how to calculate your sum of squares within, between, in total. You know how to find your F statistic. And you also know how to identify your P value. And at this point, you now know whether or not you can reject or not reject your null hypothesis.

### 14. Regression

#### [What is regression?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/what-is-regression?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/what-is-regression?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] [[Linear Regression]] analysis is a statistical attempt to understand the relationship between two variables. For example, what's the relationship between the miles on a certain type of car and the value of that car? In other words, how much value does the car lose every time I drive 500 miles? Linear regression would attempt to try and estimate the car's value knowing only the mileage on the car. We would call mileage the predictor and put it on the X axis. We would call value the outcome and put that on the Y axis. The variable on the X axis is also considered the independent variable, while the variable on the Y axis is the dependent variable. Why? Because the value of the car is dependent on the mileage of that car. So now we'd collect some data for these types of cars. Cars with different mileage, and thus different values. The data would then be plotted on a scatter chart. This is where [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] comes into play. We'll be using Excel to help us create a line that best fits our data points. Now, don't set your expectations too high. Sometimes the line and the data match up well. In other cases, the fit isn't very good. The regression line created is helpful in understanding the relationship between these two variables. Just like in algebra, your line will have a formula. It's usually in the form of y = mx + b.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/what-is-regression?u=76281980&t=97)** So as we change x on the right side of the formula, y, our dependent variable, will also change. In this formula, the letter m is the slope of our regression line. The slope of the line would indicate how a change in x would impact y. So if the slope of the line was negative 0.10, it would mean that for every 10,000 miles driven, the car loses $1,000 in value. Our line is tilting downwards as we move from left to right. In upcoming videos when we jump into Excel, we'll be performing some simple regressions. When you look at the line, what are some basic things to consider? First look at the scatterplot. Compare the regression line to the dots. Is the line a good fit? Or do the dots barely resemble the regression line? Also, what's the slope of the line? Is the line tilting upwards to the right? Is the line trending down from left to right or is the line sort of flat? In the next video, we'll look at some of the different measures we'll want to consider as we judge our regression line, and more importantly, the relationship between our variables.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] (3), [[Linear Regression]] (2)
> **Analogies:** for example (1), just like (1)

#### [Regression terminology](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/regression-terminology?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/regression-terminology?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Before we perform a regression in [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]], let's get acquainted with some terms you'll be seeing in the regression results Excel will provide you. The first thing we want to understand is the formula of our regression line, y = mx + b. Here's the formula for the regression line, y = mx + b, where b is called the intercept. If x was zero miles, y would be 21600. That's where the line intercepts or crosses the y-axis. As for the slope, it tells us how much the value of the car changes with every mile. We lose 10 cents for every mile the car drives, or the car loses $1,000 in value when the mileage goes up by 10,000 miles. Our next term is R-square, sometimes also called our coefficient of determination. R-square is the measure of how well the line fits the data points. R-square is a number between zero and one. An R-square of one means that our regression line is a perfect fit. An R-square of zero means the line is an extremely poor fit for our data points. So the better the fit, the closer the R-square is to 1.0. Another thing to consider, suppose we have an R-square of 0.75. This means that 75% of the variance
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/regression-terminology?u=76281980&t=95)** in Y is explained by X. Y is mostly changing because of X. How about the other 25% of the variance in Y? Well, it's caused by other factors, factors that are not X. Another term you'll be seeing in the regression results in Excel is significance F. Significance F is just the P-value for the regression model. Effectively, every regression exercise is a hypothesis test, a hypothesis test where the null hypothesis states that there is not a linear relationship between the data. So a low significance F is good. It means we can reject our null hypothesis. It means we have evidence that there is a linear relationship between X and Y. Next, let's look at standard error. Standard error is the average distance from the line to the dot. It's measured up and down, so it'll have the same units as the Y variable. In this case, it would be in dollars since our car value is listed in dollars. One last term to look for in our regression results is residuals. On this graph you'll see two dots. The blue dot is the actual data point, a real data point, a car with 50,000 miles sold for $20,000. But the green dot is what the regression is predicting
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/regression-terminology?u=76281980&t=193)** for that value of X. The regression model predicted a car with 50,000 miles would sell for $21,000. The difference between these two values is $1,000. The difference is called the residual. Alright, in the next video, we'll perform some regression analysis and will use these terms to examine our results.

The R-square is lower. Only 51% of a player's weight is described via the player's height. And so you might say, "Well, that seems to be lower." Yeah, because again, muscle mass, the age of the player, the position of the player, lots of different things that weren't accounted for in this particular study. And you might say, "Well, but the significance is really, really low." It's saying that there is a relationship. Now, it doesn't necessarily mean that that relationship is explaining everything, but there definitely is a relationship between height and weight. Once again, you can go look at your residuals, you can copy those down here if you wish.
>
> **[15:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/linear-regressions?u=76281980&t=922)** But otherwise, hopefully now, you have a really nice view of what Excel can do for you as it tries to do some really helpful basic linear regressions.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

> **Definitions:** is a  (5)

#### [Business simulation example](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/business-simulation-example?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/business-simulation-example?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All right, in the previous video, we learned a really basic use for Monte Carlo [[Simulation]]. In this case, we're going to do something that's slightly more difficult, slightly a little, probably a little bit more business-oriented, and hopefully a little more interesting to you. Now, the thing that you want to remember about Monte Carlo simulation is it really is all about the model that you're testing. And the more interesting the model, the more interesting the scenario, the more interesting the outcomes are going to look to you. But in this case, we're going to do something that's rather simple, but still hopefully something that interests you a little bit, especially when you start to see the results. So, what I've done in this situation, I said, "What happens if we're going to do this Monte Carlo simulation for a certain business process?" So, the process is we're going to fulfill an online order, and the online order is for three items, so somebody places an order for three different items. And so, we have to pick the correct three items off the shelf from the warehouse. We then have to deliver them to that person's home. If there's an error in any one of those operations, well, obviously there's going to be a problem with that specific order, 'cause either we delivered it to the wrong place, or they got the wrong item number one or the wrong item number two, or maybe there were two errors in that particular situation. Nonetheless, let's go ahead and look at this. And what I've done is I'm going to establish a reliability rate. So, out of a hundred,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-excel-statistics-with-copilot/business-simulation-example?u=76281980&t=93)** how many times will they get this right? And I said, "Let's say that they're going to get it right 99 out of a hundred times." Nothing is a hundred percent reliable, but let's go with 99. So, it's pretty high. Our error rate is just going to be one minus the reliability rate. And what we're going to do is we're going to make this a random model. So, we're going to say we're going to choose a number between one and a hundred. And as long as this number isn't equal to or smaller than our error rate, then we should be fine. So, in this case here, we're fine. The random number is three, we're good. Let's roll it again. Now it's nine, that's still good. And do it again and again. And you can see that it's looking pretty good for us. Now, what I did was I set up a test over here and you can see my formula, you might say, "Well, what is that formula doing?" It's saying that if the random number is less than our error rate plus one, so our error rate is 1%, plus one, so two. As long as this number here isn't smaller than two, we're going to be okay. And so, if this comes up as zero, we're okay. If it comes up as one, well, now it's a defect. And I'm going to do this for all four of my items, for all four of my operations.
